إلى وقوع الشروع خاليا عنها (ألبوم تاير قص: ۶۷) اوپر کے مذکورہ مسائل سے یہ بات معلوم ہوگئی کہ نیت میں قرآن حقیقی یعنی نماز شروع کرتے وقت یا پھر قرآن حکمی مینی پایگی بیت کا ہونا ضروری ہے ۔ والنية هي العلم السابق بالعمل اللاحق ويجوز تقديمها على التكبيرة و إذا لم يوجد ما يقطعها وهو عمل لايليق بالصلاة (الجوهرة التيزة ج ١ ، ص : ٢٧)
If such an act is found between the intention and the commencement of prayer that is not of the genre of prayer, even then the prayer becomes valid. The issue is that if someone performed ablution at home, intended the prayer, and walked to the mosque and joined the imam or prayed alone, the prayer is valid, although walking is an act not of the genre of prayer. The answer is that the meaning of 'not of the genre' is that the act indicates turning away, like speaking or eating, but walking to prayer does not indicate that, so it is counted among the acts of prayer and is not a breaker. The conclusion: according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), commencing prayer and all acts of worship is valid with a prior intention if one does not engage after it in another act unsuitable for prayer. (Tatar Khaniyya, vol. 2, p. 435) In al-Khulasa: Our companions are unanimous that it is best for the intention to be concurrent with the commencement, and one should not commence with a delayed intention, because what has passed is not an act of worship due to lack of intention; similarly the remaining part due to lack of apportionment. Translation: And in al-Khulasa, our companions are unanimous that the best is that the intention be concurrent with the commencement, because what has passed is not worship due to lack of intention, so similarly the rest due to lack of division. Explanation: All our companions agree that it is best for the intention to be concurrent with the start, although delayed intention has also been deemed permissible. In al-Anfa': The principle regarding intention is that it should be concurrent except when necessary, as in fasting. (Tatar Khaniyya, vol. 1, p. 235) If a person performs an action and had no intention at the start, and intended after the takbir, then according to the apparent narration this intention is not valid. And if the intention is delayed from the commencement, such that intention was absent at the time of commencement, and he intended after the takbir, then in the apparent narration it is not valid. (Tatar Khaniyya, vol. 1, p. 435) Ibn Wahban reported a disagreement among the mashayikh outside the school, agreeing with what was reported from al-Karkhi about the permissibility of delay after the tahrimah. It was said: up to the thana, up to the ta'awwudh, up to the ruku', up to the raising; all are weak, and the relied-upon position is that concurrency (haqiqatan or hukman) is necessary. In al-Jawhara: The statement of al-Karkhi is not considered. Translation: Ibn Wahban reported a disagreement among the mashayikh outside the school, agreeing with what was reported from al-Karkhi about the permissibility of delay after the tahrimah. Thus one saying is to the thana, one to the ta'awwudh, one to the ruku', one to the raising, and all these sayings are weak, and the relied-upon is that it is necessary that there be concurrency. In al-Jawhara: The statement of al-Karkhi is not considered. Explanation: This has been mentioned before that delayed intention is not considered. Now it is being explained that all these sayings are weak and outside the school, and among them there is disagreement as to up to when it is considered. Some said up to the end of thana, some to ta'awwudh, some to ruku', some to standing after ruku'. But all are weak. And the delayed intention after the tahrimah is not considered because what has passed is not worship due to lack of intention. Al-Karkhi permitted a delayed intention at the tahrimah, but they differed: up to when? Some said to the end of thana, some to ta'awwudh. The statement of al-Karkhi is not considered because intention after commencement leads to the commencement being empty of it. (Al-Multaqat? p. 67) From the above issues it becomes known that in intention, real concurrency (at the start of prayer) or ruling concurrency is necessary. And intention is prior knowledge of the subsequent action, and it is permissible to precede it to the takbir if nothing cuts it off, which is an act unsuitable for prayer. (Al-Jawharat al-Nayyira, vol. 1, p. 27)
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the timing of intention (niyyah) in prayer according to the Hanafi school. It begins by addressing a potential objection: if a person makes intention at home and then walks to the mosque, that walking is not an act of prayer, so does it break the continuity? The answer is that walking to the mosque does not indicate turning away from prayer; rather it is part of the prayer's preparation. Thus it does not invalidate the prior intention. The passage then summarizes Imam Muhammad's position that commencing prayer or any worship with a prior intention is valid provided no action unsuitable to that worship intervenes. The word 'unsuitable' here means an act that indicates disengagement, like talking or eating. After that, the passage relays the consensus from al-Khulasa that the best is for the intention to be concurrent (muqarinah) with the start, but it does not absolutely reject the validity of a delayed intention. It then quotes a principle from al-Anfa': 'The original rule in intention is that it should be concurrent except when necessary, as in fasting.' This is a key qawida. Next, it clarifies the apparently transmitted position (Zahir al-Riwaya): if intention is delayed (i.e., one forgets to intend at the start and intends after the takbir), the prayer is not valid. This is the strict view. However, other opinions from some later mashayikh, reported by Ibn Wahban, allowed delay up to certain points (thana, ta'awwudh, ruku', etc.). These are considered weak and outside the school. The relied-upon position is that concurrency—whether real or ruling (hukman)—is necessary. Al-Karkhi's opinion has no weight. The discussion ends by emphasizing that intention must be either real concurrency (at the moment of takbir) or ruling concurrency (where the prior intention is not vitiated by an interrupting act).

Key Terms
النية المتقدمة (al-niyyah al-mutaqaddimah): A prior intention made before the actual commencement of an act of worship, which remains valid if no interrupting action occurs.
المقارنة الحقيقية (al-muqāranah al-ḥaqīqiyyah): Real concurrency: the intention is present at the exact moment of commencing the act.
المقارنة الحكمية (al-muqāranah al-ḥukmiyyah): Ruling concurrency: the intention was made prior but is considered concurrent as long as no interrupting act occurred.
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The transmitted position from the core Hanafi books (Imam Muhammad's six works), which strictly requires concurrency and invalidates delayed intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the qawida in al-Anfa', what is the default ruling on intention in worship?
A) It should be delayed as much as possible.
B) It should be concurrent except when necessity arises.
C) It must always be made at the exact moment of commencement.
D) It can be made any time before the act.

Key Takeaway
The original rule is that intention must be concurrent with the act; necessity allows prior intention, but any unsuitable act in between breaks it.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
10 min
The Mufta Bihi on Delayed Intention: Rejecting Al-Karkhi's View
Dispute on delayed intention and tarjih of concurrency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relied-upon Hanafi position on delayed intention, explain why al-Karkhi's view is not accepted, and apply the rule that concurrency (real or ruling) is necessary.

Core Idea
A delayed intention after the takbir invalidates prayer; only concurrency, real or ruling, is valid.

This lesson focuses on the intra-Hanafi dispute about whether intention can be delayed after the opening takbir. While some opinions allowed delay up to various points, the relied-upon position requires concurrency. We will examine the evidence, the authority levels, and the practical fatwa that a mufti must give.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue and the Zahir al-Riwayah Position
The issue is: if a person forgets to intend at the start and intends after the takbir, is the prayer valid? Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmitted position) states it is not valid. This is found in Tatar Khaniyya and is the strongest narration.
Zahir al-Riwayah represents the six works of Imam Muhammad.
2
Present Al-Karkhi's Position and Its Variations
Al-Karkhi and some later mashayikh outside the school allowed a delayed intention up to certain points: the thana, ta'awwudh, ruku', or raising from ruku'. These are reported by Ibn Wahban.
These opinions are considered weak (da'if) and outside the madhhab (kharij 'an al-madhhab).
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response to Al-Karkhi
The Hanafi response: The analogy with fasting is flawed because fasting spans the whole day, while prayer has a specific start. The consensus from al-Khulasa requires concurrency. Also, allowing delay would mean part of the prayer was performed without intention, which cannot become worship.
The response cites ijma and the principle that what passed without intention cannot be rectified later.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi (Relied-upon Position)
The mu'tamad is that concurrency is necessary, either haqiqatan (real) or hukman (ruling). The statement of al-Karkhi is not considered (la mu'tabara bi qawl al-Karkhi) as per al-Jawhara.
This is the ruling for fatwa today.
5
Apply the Ruling to a Practical Scenario
If a mufti is asked about a person who intended after the takbir, the answer must be: the prayer is invalid and must be repeated. The only exception is if he had a prior intention that remained unbroken (ruling concurrency).
The mufti must be careful not to give fatwa based on weak opinions.

If such an act is found between the intention and the commencement of prayer that is not of the genre of prayer, even then the prayer becomes valid. The issue is that if someone performed ablution at home, intended the prayer, and walked to the mosque and joined the imam or prayed alone, the prayer is valid, although walking is an act not of the genre of prayer. The answer is that the meaning of 'not of the genre' is that the act indicates turning away, like speaking or eating, but walking to prayer does not indicate that, so it is counted among the acts of prayer and is not a breaker. The conclusion: according to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), commencing prayer and all acts of worship is valid with a prior intention if one does not engage after it in another act unsuitable for prayer. (Tatar Khaniyya, vol. 2, p. 435) In al-Khulasa: Our companions are unanimous that it is best for the intention to be concurrent with the commencement, and one should not commence with a delayed intention, because what has passed is not an act of worship due to lack of intention; similarly the remaining part due to lack of apportionment. Translation: And in al-Khulasa, our companions are unanimous that the best is that the intention be concurrent with the commencement, because what has passed is not worship due to lack of intention, so similarly the rest due to lack of division. Explanation: All our companions agree that it is best for the intention to be concurrent with the start, although delayed intention has also been deemed permissible. In al-Anfa': The principle regarding intention is that it should be concurrent except when necessary, as in fasting. (Tatar Khaniyya, vol. 1, p. 235) If a person performs an action and had no intention at the start, and intended after the takbir, then according to the apparent narration this intention is not valid. And if the intention is delayed from the commencement, such that intention was absent at the time of commencement, and he intended after the takbir, then in the apparent narration it is not valid. (Tatar Khaniyya, vol. 1, p. 435) Ibn Wahban reported a disagreement among the mashayikh outside the school, agreeing with what was reported from al-Karkhi about the permissibility of delay after the tahrimah. In al-Jawhara: The statement of al-Karkhi is not considered. Translation: Ibn Wahban reported a disagreement among the mashayikh outside the school, agreeing with what was reported from al-Karkhi about the permissibility of delay after the tahrimah. Thus one saying is to the thana, one to the ta'awwudh, one to the ruku', one to the raising, and all these sayings are weak, and the relied-upon is that it is necessary that there be concurrency. In al-Jawhara: The statement of al-Karkhi is not considered. Explanation: This has been mentioned before that delayed intention is not considered. Now it is being explained that all these sayings are weak and outside the school, and among them there is disagreement as to up to when it is considered. Some said up to the end of thana, some to ta'awwudh, some to ruku', some to standing after ruku'. But all are weak. And the delayed intention after the tahrimah is not considered because what has passed is not worship due to lack of intention. The statement of al-Karkhi is not considered because intention after commencement leads to the commencement being empty of it. (Al-Multaqat? p. 67) From the above issues it becomes known that in intention, real concurrency (at the start of prayer) or ruling concurrency is necessary. And intention is prior knowledge of the subsequent action, and it is permissible to precede it to the takbir if nothing cuts it off, which is an act unsuitable for prayer. (Al-Jawharat al-Nayyira, vol. 1, p. 27)

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the timing of intention (niyyah) in prayer according to the Hanafi school. It begins by addressing a potential objection: if a person makes intention at home and then walks to the mosque, that walking is not an act of prayer, so does it break the continuity? The answer is that walking to the mosque does not indicate turning away from prayer; rather it is part of the prayer's preparation. Thus it does not invalidate the prior intention. The word 'unsuitable' here means an act that indicates disengagement, like talking or eating. It then quotes a principle from al-Anfa': 'The original rule in intention is that it should be concurrent except when necessary, as in fasting.' This is a key qawida. Next, it clarifies the apparently transmitted position (Zahir al-Riwaya): if intention is delayed (i.e., one forgets to intend at the start and intends after the takbir), the prayer is not valid. These are considered weak and outside the school. The relied-upon position is that concurrency—whether real or ruling (hukman)—is necessary. Al-Karkhi's opinion has no weight. The discussion ends by emphasizing that intention must be either real concurrency (at the moment of takbir) or ruling concurrency (where the prior intention is not vitiated by an interrupting act).

النية المتقدمة (al-niyyah al-mutaqaddimah): A prior intention made before the actual commencement of an act of worship, which remains valid if no interrupting action occurs.
المقارنة الحقيقية (al-muqāranah al-ḥaqīqiyyah): Real concurrency: the intention is present at the exact moment of commencing the act.
المقارنة الحكمية (al-muqāranah al-ḥukmiyyah): Ruling concurrency: the intention was made prior but is considered concurrent as long as no interrupting act occurred.

المعتمد (al-mu'tamad): The relied-upon position in the school, used for fatwa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Invalidity of Later Intention in Prayer
Stating the rule that intention after takbir is invalid and rejecting all weak opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mufta bihi ruling on the timing of intention in prayer and apply the rasm al-mufti principle that weak opinions are not relied upon.

Core Idea
Intention must coincide with the opening takbir; any later intention invalidates the prayer's beginning.

The timing of intention (niyyah) in prayer is a critical condition for its validity. This lesson establishes the authoritative Hanafi position: intention must be present at the moment of the opening takbir (tahrimah). It also addresses a weak opinion attributed to al-Karkhi and explains why it cannot be acted upon. Understanding this ruling is essential for any mufti or student of fiqh when giving fatwa on prayer conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the ruling concerns the intention (niyyah) for prayer. The text states that an intention occurring after the takbir is invalid.
The key is that the beginning of prayer must be an intentional act of worship.
2
Locate the weak opinion
Note that al-Karkhi allowed a later intention at the moment of takbir, and some scholars extended the cutoff to thana' or ta'awwudh.
These views are not considered in the madhhab's relied-upon position.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The response is clear: if intention comes after starting, the first part of prayer occurs without intention, which invalidates the act.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the necessity of intention from the start.
4
Learn the definition of niyyah
The text defines niyyah as 'prior knowledge of the subsequent action,' meaning it must precede the action or be simultaneous with its start.
This definition reinforces the timing requirement.
5
Note the condition of prior intention
Intention can be made before the takbir, provided there is no interruption by an action unsuitable to prayer.
If interrupted, the intention must be renewed at the takbir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا معتبر بالمتاخرة عند التحريمة لان ما مضى لا يقع عبادة ..... لعدم النية و عند الكرخي يجوز بنية متاخرة عند التحريمة واختلفوا إلى أن قال بعضهم إلى منتهى الثناء قيل إلى التعوذ ولا يعتبر بقول الكرخي لان النية بعد الشروع تودى إلى وقوع الشروع خاليا عنها. والنية هي العلم السابق بالعمل اللاحق ويجوز تقديمها على التكبيرة و إذا لم يوجد ما يقطعها وهو عمل لا يليق بالصلاة.
And there is no consideration for the intention that comes after the takbir, because what has passed cannot occur as worship due to lack of intention. And according to al-Karkhi, it is permissible with a later intention at the takbir. And they differed; some said until the end of the thana', it was said until the ta'awwudh. And the statement of al-Karkhi is not considered, because intention after starting leads to the occurrence of the start devoid of it. And intention is the prior knowledge of the subsequent action, and it is permissible to bring it forward before the takbir if nothing cuts it off, and it is an action that is not suitable for prayer.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the required timing of intention (niyyah) in prayer. The author establishes that intention must be present at the very moment of the opening takbir (tahrimah). Any intention that comes later is invalid, because the beginning of the prayer would then be without intention. This is the authoritative Hanafi position. Although some scholars like al-Karkhi allowed a late intention, and others debated the cutoff point (e.g., until the end of thana' or ta'awwudh), all such views are considered weak and outside the madhhab. The author then defines niyyah as 'prior knowledge of the subsequent action' and notes it can be made before the takbir as long as it remains uninterrupted.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): Prior knowledge of the subsequent action; mental determination to perform a specific act of worship.
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir that makes prohibited what was previously permissible.
المتاخرة (al-muta'akhirah): Intention that comes after the beginning of the opening takbir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Any time before the first rak'ah of prayer
C) During the recitation of thana'
D) Before the takbir, even if interrupted

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, intention for prayer must coincide with the opening takbir; any later intention invalidates the prayer, and weak opinions to the contrary are not relied upon in fatwa.

Tawaf vs. Wuquf: The Need for Independent Intention
Explains why tawaf requires independent intention while wuquf does not when chasing a debtor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the distinction between independent and non-independent acts of worship (qurbat mustaqilla vs. ghayr mustaqilla) to determine when a separate intention is required for a ritual act, using tawaf and wuquf as a case study.

Core Idea
An independent worship requires its own intention; a non-independent act is covered by the general intention of ihram.

In this lesson, we examine a key distinction in Hanafi jurisprudence between acts of worship that are independent (qurbat mustaqilla) and those that are not. The example given is tawaf vs. wuquf: if someone performs them while chasing a debtor, tawaf is invalid without a separate intention, but wuquf is valid. Understanding this distinction helps muftis evaluate similar mixed-intention scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and rulings
Read the passage and note that performing tawaf while chasing a debtor does not fulfill the obligation, but performing wuquf similarly does. Identify the acts and their outcomes.
The key contrast is that both acts are from Hajj rituals but ruled differently.
2
Classify tawaf as qurbat mustaqilla
Recognize that tawaf is considered an independent act of worship (qurbat mustaqilla). This means it has its own identity and requires a specific intention (niyya) separate from the general intention of ihram.
Wuquf is not classified as such; it is a ritual that derives its validity from the overarching intention of ihram.
3
Analyze Zayla'i's second distinction
Consider the timing: tawaf occurs partly after leaving ihram (halal) and partly in ihram, so the original ihram intention does not fully encompass it. Wuquf is entirely within ihram, so the ihram intention covers it.
This explanation reinforces why independent intention is needed for tawaf.
4
Apply to new cases
As a mufti, when faced with a mixed-intention scenario, ask: Is the act considered qurbat mustaqilla? Is it fully within a state that already has a valid intention (e.g., ihram)? If yes to first and no to second, separate intention is required.
Example: Sa'i between Safa and Marwa – scholars differ on whether it is qurbat mustaqilla; this ruling guides the fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه: فقہاء کرام فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے قرضدار کا پیچھا کرتے ہوئے طواف کیا تو وہ اس کے لئے کافی نہیں ہے اور اگر اسی طرح عرفات میں ٹھہرا تو وہ اس کے لئے کافی ہے اور اسے ہم پہلے بیان کر چکے ہیں اور فرق یہ ہے کہ طواف کو قربت مستقلہ جانا گیا ہے برخلاف وقوف کے اور زیلعی نے ان دونوں کے مابین ایک دوسرا فرق کیا ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ احرام کے وقت کی نیت ان تمام امور کو شامل ہے جو احرام کی حالت میں انجام دیئے جاتے ہیں تو تجدید نیت کی ضرورت نہیں اور طواف من وجہ حلال ہونے کے بعد ہوتا ہے اور من وجہ حالت احرام میں اس لئے اس میں اصل نیت شروط ہے نہ کہ جہت کی تعیین زیلعی کا کلام پورا ہوا۔ تشریح: یہ دونوں مسئلے ماقبل میں گذر چکے ہیں یہاں ان کے درمیان فرق بیان کیا جارہا ہے جن کا حکم الگ الگ ہے اور قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ ہے کہ دونوں مسئلوں کا حکم ایک ہی ہونا چاہئے اور وہ مسئلے یہ ہیں کہ اگر اس نے اپنے مقروض کو تلاش کرنے کی غرض سے وقوف عرفہ کیا تو وقوف عرفہ بیج ہو جائے گا۔ ان دونوں کے مابین ایک فرق تو یہ ہے کہ طواف قربت مستقلہ ہے جس میں تحصیل ضروری ہے لہذا اس کے لئے مستقل نیت کی ضرورت ہے۔ جکہ وقوف عرفہ قربت مستقلہ نہیں بلکہ اس کا حصول ضروری ہے اس لئے اس کے لئے مستقل نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔ (۲) دوسرا فریق علامہ زیلعی نے ذکر کیا ہے جو در حقیقت اس کی وضاحت ہے کہ احترام کے وقت کی نیت ان تمام افعال کو شامل ہوتی ہے جو بندہ احرام کے اندر کرتا ہے لہذا تجد ید نیت کی اسے ضرورت نہیں رہتی اور طواف حلال ہونے کے بعد ہوتا ہے اور احترام میں من وجہ ہوتا ہے اس لئے احرام کے وقت کی نیت اس جگہ پورے طور پر موجود نہ رہی جس کی وجہ سے احرام میں اصل نیت کو تو ضروری قرار دیا گیا ہے، تعیین جہت نہیں تو خوان بدون نیت کے ہوا طواف احترام والی نیت کے ضمن میں نہیں ہے۔

Zayla'i's statement ends. Explanation: These two issues have passed before; here the difference between them is explained; their rulings are different, and analogy would require them to be same. The issues are: if he stood in Arafat to find his debtor, his wuquf is valid. One difference: tawaf is an independent worship needing attainment, so independent intention is required. Wuquf is not independent; its attainment is necessary, so independent intention is not required. (2) The second difference mentioned by Allama Zayla'i actually clarifies that the intention at ihram includes all actions done in ihram, so no renewal needed. Tawaf occurs partly after halal and partly in ihram, so the intention at ihram is not fully present, thus original intention is required but not specification of direction.

Commentary
This passage presents a legal rule (rasm) regarding the necessity of independent intention in worship. The issue is whether an act of worship performed incidentally (e.g., while chasing a debtor) fulfills the religious obligation. The rule distinguishes tawaf from wuquf: tawaf is an independent act of worship (qurbat mustaqilla) requiring its own intention, while wuquf is not. Therefore, if one performs tawaf while pursuing a debtor, it is invalid because the intention was not for worship; but wuquf in such a state is valid because the ihram intention covers it. A second distinction by Zayla'i: ihram intention covers all acts during ihram, but tawaf occurs partly after leaving ihram, so it needs its own intention. This rule guides muftis in evaluating similar cases where worship is mixed with worldly purposes.

Key Terms
قربت مستقلہ (qurbat mustaqilla): An act of worship that is an independent ritual in itself, requiring a specific intention apart from the general intention of ihram.
وقوف (wuquf): Standing at Arafat on the 9th of Dhul Hijja, a pillar of Hajj.

Place of Intention: Heart is the Seat, Exceptions for Inability and Forgetfulness
Defines the seat of intention as the heart and the rules for verbal utterance and forgetfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that intention's place is the heart, identify the exceptions for verbalization and forgetfulness, and apply them in fatwa.

Core Idea
Intention resides in the heart; verbal utterance is not a substitute, but incapacity and forgetfulness are excused.

This lesson unpacks a foundational qawida in Hanafi fiqh: the place (mahall) of intention is the heart. The text establishes that mere verbal expression without heart presence is insufficient, yet provides practical concessions for those who cannot concentrate or who act forgetfully. As a mufti, understanding this principle and its exceptions is crucial for issuing sound rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qawida
The core principle: intention's place is the heart in all situations. Recognize this as a definitive rule (qa'idah) governing the validity of worship.
The text states: 'Its place is the heart in every situation' (محلها القلب في كل موضع).
2
Analyze the First Exception
The first sub-rule: one who cannot bring the heart to intention, or doubts, may verbalize; the verbalization suffices. This is based on the Qur'anic principle of no burden beyond capacity (2:286).
Exception applies only when genuine incapacity or doubt exists.
3
Analyze the Second Exception
The second sub-rule: forgetfulness (sahw) does not invalidate worship; the prayer is valid, though reward for that part is not earned.
Forgetfulness is a natural human condition and is forgiven.
4
Determine the Dalil
The proof for the exceptions is the Qur'anic verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286), cited in the text.
This dalil is used by Hanafi jurists to justify concessions in intention.
5
Apply in Fatwa
In practice, a mufti rules that intention must be in the heart, but if a person is unable to concentrate or forgets, the worship remains valid.
The mufta bihi is the principle with the exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّاسِعُ فِي مَحَلَّهَا : مَحَلُّهَا الْقَلْبُ فِي كُلِّ مَوْضِعِ، وَقَدَّمْنَا حَقِيْقَتَهَا، وَهَهُنَا أصْلَانِ الأصْلُ الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ كَا يَكْفِي التَّلَفظُ بِاللسان دُونَهُ، وَفِي الْقِنْيَةِ وَ" الْمُجْتَبى وَمَنْ لَا يَقْدِرُ أَنْ يُحْضِرَ قَلْبَهُ لِيَنْوِي بِقَلْبِهِ، أَوْ يَشُكُ فِي النِّيَّةِ يَكْفِيهِ التَّكَلَّمُ بِلِسَانِهِ؛ لأنَّهُ لَا يَكَلَّفُ اللهُ نَفْساً إلَّا وُسْعَهَا إِنْتَهَى ثُمَّ قَالَ فِيْهَا : وَلَا يُؤْاخَذْ يا النِّيَّةِ حَالَ سَهْوِهِ؛ لِأَنَّ مَا يَفْعَلُهُ مِنَ الصَّلَاةِ فِيْمَا يَسْهُمْ مَعْفُوٌّ عَنْهُ، وَصَلَاتُهُ مُجْزِنَةٌ وَإِنْ لَّمْ يَسْتَحِقْ بِهَا ثَوَابِاً، إِنْتَهَى.
ترجمه نویس بحث اس ( نیت) کے محل کے بیان میں ہے اس کا محل ہر مقام میں دل ہے اور ہم اس کی حقیقت کو بیان کر چکے ہیں یہاں دوضابطے ہیں پہلا ضابطہ دل کے بغیر زبان سے تلفظ کرنا کافی نہیں ہے اور قنیہ دمجتبی میں ہے کہ جو اپنے قلب کے حاضر کرنے پر قادر نہ ہوتا کہ اپنے قلب سے نیت کرے یا نیت میں شک کرے تو اس کے لئے زبان سے تکلم کرنا کافی ہے اس لئے کہ اللہ تعالیٰ کسی کو اس کی طاقت سے زیادہ کا مکلف نہیں بناتا بات پوری ہوگی پھر اسی میں کہا ہے کہ ہو کی حالت میں نیت پر مواخذہ نہیں کیا جائے گا اس لئے کہ نماز میں سہو کی حالت میں جودہ کرنا ہے معاف ہے اور اس کی نماز کافی ہے اگر چہ وہ اس کے ثواب کا مستحق نہیں ہوگا بات پوری ہوئی۔
The one who gives detailed rulings on the place of intention: its place is the heart in every situation, and we have already presented its reality. Here are two principles: The first principle: That mere verbal utterance with the tongue without the heart is not sufficient. In al-Qunya and al-Mujtaba: One who is unable to bring his heart to intend with his heart, or who doubts in intention, then speaking with his tongue is sufficient for him, because Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. Then he said in it: And he will not be taken to account for intention in a state of forgetfulness; because what he does of prayer in forgetfulness is forgiven, and his prayer is valid even if he does not deserve reward. End. (Urdu:) The author says that this discussion is about the place of intention. Its place in every situation is the heart, and we have explained its reality. And in al-Qunya and al-Mujtaba: The one who is not able to bring his heart to intend with his heart, or who doubts in intention, then for him speaking with his tongue is sufficient, because Allah does not burden anyone beyond his capacity. Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines the locus (mahall) of intention in the Hanafi school. It clarifies that intention is fundamentally an act of the heart, not the tongue. Merely uttering words without heart presence is insufficient for a valid intention. However, exceptions apply: (1) If a person cannot concentrate his heart (e.g., due to extreme distraction) or is in doubt about his intention, verbalizing the intention suffices. This is based on the Qur'anic principle 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286). (2) In cases of forgetfulness (sahw), a person is not held accountable for the absence of intention during the act. His prayer is valid (mujzi'a), though he does not earn reward for that portion. This is because forgetfulness is a natural human condition and is forgiven. The two principles (aslan) guide muftis: the heart intention is necessary, but pragmatic concessions exist for genuine incapacity and forgetfulness.

Key Terms
محل النية (mahall al-niyyah): The location where intention resides; this text establishes it exclusively as the heart (qalb) in all contexts.
سهو (sahw): Forgetfulness, a state where one is not consciously aware of the intention; the text rules it is forgiven and does not invalidate worship.
تلفظ (talfaz): Verbal utterance of the intention; the text states it is not sufficient if the heart is not present, except for those unable to concentrate.

Key Takeaway
The seat of intention is the heart; verbalization alone is insufficient, but incapacity and forgetfulness are excused by the principle of no burden beyond capacity.

The Ruling on Calling a Slave 'Free' and the Mahbubi Distinction
Critical refinement of a ruling: Mahbubi's distinction is rejected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that knowledge of the addressee suffices for legal effects, and evaluate Mahbubi's distinction between talaq and 'itaq in light of the mashhur view.

Core Idea
Calling a slave 'Free' frees him if intention is present; the mashhur view rejects Mahbubi's distinction.

This passage presents a classic scenario: a slave's given name is 'Free' and his master calls him by that name. Does he become free? The initial ruling says no, but the discussion reveals a deeper scholarly debate. Imam Mahbubi distinguished this case from divorce, arguing that calling a wife 'Taliq' results in divorce while calling a slave 'Free' does not free him. However, the well-known (mashhur) view among the majority of Hanafi scholars rejects this distinction. Understanding this debate trains a mufti to recognize when a textual principle (knowledge of the addressee) overrides a superficial difference in terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Initial Ruling and Its Context
Read the first line of the passage: if a slave's name is 'Free' and he is called by that name, he does not become free. Note that this ruling applies when the caller does not necessarily intend emancipation.
This is the surface ruling that sets up the discussion.
2
Understand Mahbubi's Distinction
Mahbubi argued that calling a wife 'Taliq' or 'Mutallaqah' constitutes divorce because these are actual names for a divorced woman, but 'Free' is not a name given to a slave, so calling a slave 'Free' is only kinayah (implicit) and requires intention to free.
Mahbubi's reasoning is based on the nature of the term (proper name vs. description).
3
Analyze Ibn Nujaym's Critique
Ibn Nujaym cites al-Bahr al-Ra'iq stating that most Hanafi books do not differentiate between talaq and 'itaq. The condition for effectiveness is that the addressee is known (ma'hud), not that the term is a proper name.
The key principle: 'ilm (knowledge) of the person being addressed suffices.
4
Determine the Mashhur (Well-Known) View
The mashhur view is that there is no distinction: if the caller intends divorce or emancipation and the addressee is identified, the effect occurs. This is the mufta bihi position for fatwa.
This view is supported by the majority of Hanafi sources.
5
Apply the Principle to a New Scenario
Consider: a master calls his slave 'Free' intending emancipation. According to the mashhur view, the slave becomes free. The principle is that 'ilm (knowing whom you are addressing) is sufficient, regardless of whether the term is a proper name.
This step reinforces the practical application of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طرح اگر کسی کے غلام کا نام حر تھا اور اس نے یا حر سے پکارا تو وہ آزاد نہیں ہوگا۔ علامہ ابن نجیم المصری فرماتے ہیں کہ اس سلسلہ میں محبوبی نے طلاق و عناق کے درمیان فرق کرنے کے بعد جو یہ بات کہی ہے کہ طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور غلام آزاد نہیں ہوگا ، وہ فقہاء کے قول مشہور کے خلاف ہے۔ وكذا في الطلاق و تحته فى الشامية قال فى البحر و فى اكثر الكتب لم يفرق بينهما لان العلم لم يشترط فيه ان يكون معهوداً والكلام فيما اذا اشهد وقت التسمية فيهما فالظاهر عدم الفرق (شامی زکریاج ۵ : ۳۸۵)
علامہ محبوبی نے دونوں مسئلوں میں جو فرق کیا ہے کہ طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اور محقق واقع نہ ہوگی کیونکہ تر نام رکھا جاتا ہے اور مطلقہ یا طالق نام نہیں رکھا جاتا ہے۔ تو محبوبی کا یہ فرق کرنا درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ علم میں معبود ہونا شرم نہیں اور انہوں نے فرق کی بنیاد اسی کو قرار دیا ہے۔
First: If someone's slave's name was 'Free' and he called him or called him by 'free', he will not become free. Allamah Ibn Nujaym al-Misri says that Mahbubi, after differentiating between divorce and emancipation, said that divorce will occur and the slave will not become free, which is against the well-known saying of the jurists. And similarly in divorce, in al-Shamiyyah it is said in al-Bahr: 'In most books they did not differentiate between them because knowledge ('ilm) does not require being well-known (ma'hud), and the discussion is regarding when he witnesses at the time of naming, so apparently there is no difference.' (Shami, Zakariya 5:385)
Allamah Mahbubi made a distinction between the two issues that divorce will occur and emancipation will not occur because the name is kept and a divorced woman or 'taliq' name is not kept. So this distinction of Mahbubi is not correct because knowledge ('ilm) is not something that requires being well-known (ma'hud), and he based the distinction on that.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and a critique. It begins by stating a ruling: if a slave's given name is 'Free' and someone addresses him with that name, he does not become free. Allamah Ibn Nujaym then reports that Imam Mahbubi made a distinction between this case and a case of divorce: if someone calls his wife by the name 'Taliq' or 'Mutallaqah', divorce would occur (because these are actual names for a divorced woman), but calling a slave by the name 'Free' does not free him because 'free' is not a name given to a slave to indicate his status. Ibn Nujaym criticizes this distinction, citing the view in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that most Hanafi books do not differentiate, because the condition for the effectiveness of such expressions is that the addressee is known (ma'hud), not that the term is necessarily a proper name. The passage teaches that when the name is given by a third party and the caller intends to free or divorce by calling the name, it is the intention and the fact that the addressee is known that matters, not the technicality of whether it is a proper name. The correct view, according to the mas'hur, is that calling a slave 'free' would free him if the caller intends emancipation, but if the name is merely a label from others, it does not. Here, the well-known position is that there is no difference between divorce and emancipation in this regard; both are effective if the addressee is known and the intention is there. The mashayikh reject Mahbubi's differentiation.

Key Terms
معهود (ma‘hūd): Here, it means that the person being addressed must be known (identified) at the time the name or expression is used, but this knowledge does not have to be from common custom or prior acquaintance.
علم (‘ilm): In this context, it refers to the knowledge of whom the speaker is addressing, i.e., the identification of the person being called by the name or description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known Hanafi view (mashhūr), what is the condition for a statement like calling a person 'Free' or 'Taliq' to be effective in emancipation or divorce?
A) The term must be a proper name.
B) The addressee must be known (ma‘hūd) and the caller must intend the legal effect.
C) The caller must explicitly say 'I free you' or 'I divorce you.'
D) The witness must be present at the time of naming.

Key Takeaway
The mashhūr Hanafi view does not distinguish between talaq and 'itaq when calling a person by a name indicating that status; both are effective if the addressee is known and intention is present.

Intention and General Divorce: Diyānah vs. Qaḍā’ and the Muḥallifah Case
Multiple rulings on general statements, intention, and the diyānah/qaḍā’ distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between diyānah and qaḍā’ to general divorce statements and identify when a specific intention is accepted or rejected in each sphere.

Core Idea
A general divorce includes all wives; a bare intention to exclude is not accepted in court but may be between him and Allah, except when context gives a strong presumption.

This passage illustrates a central principle in Hanafi fatwa methodology: the boundary between what a person claims privately (diyānah) and what a judge enforces (qaḍā’). It presents several rulings on the statement 'kullu imra'ati li taliq' and the husband's subsequent claim of intention to exclude a specific wife. The mufti must understand when such intention is accepted and when it is not, and how the context of the utterance (e.g., responding to a wife's question) can shift the ruling. We will unpack these rulings to derive the governing rules for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Start by noting that when a husband says 'kullu imra'ati li taliq' and later claims he intended to exclude a particular wife, the default Hanafi rule is that his intention is not accepted at all – neither diyānah nor qaḍā’. The general wording is explicit and cannot be overridden by a hidden intention.
This establishes a strict rule: bare intention does not restrict a general pronouncement.
2
Distinguish the muḥallifah scenario
Now examine the case where the wife asks 'Did you marry over me?' and the husband replies with the same general statement. Here, two Hanafi narrations exist: one that the interrogating wife is divorced, and another (Abu Yusuf) that she is not. The key difference is that the husband's words are a direct response to her question, creating a strong contextual presumption (qarīnah) that he only meant the other wife.
Context can create a legal presumption that restricts the general meaning, even overriding the literal scope.
3
Apply the tarjīḥ (preferred view)
Note that the muftī's position is the narration of Abu Yusuf, as chosen by al-Sarakhsī and most mashāyikh. This means in practice, the interrogating wife is not divorced, and this holds both diyānah and qaḍā’ (the text implies acceptance in both, though it explicitly mentions diyānah for similar cases later).
The preference is based on the strength of the contextual qarīnah.
4
Extend the principle to manumission
The passage extends the analysis to manumission: 'kullu mamlūkī li ḥurrun' frees all slaves. If the owner intends only males, he is believed diyānan (takhṣīṣ al-‘āmm). But if he intends only black slaves or only women, he is not believed even diyānan, because that is takhṣīṣ al-waṣf – specification of an attribute not inherent in the general word.
This highlights the limit: intention can specify the general only if the specification is within the scope of the general category, not an external description.
5
Formulate the rule for fatwa
Synthesize: When a general statement is made, the muftī must look for contextual qarā'in (indications) that may restrict it. Absent strong context, a bare intention is accepted only diyānan (not in court) for takhṣīṣ al-‘āmm, but never for takhṣīṣ al-waṣf. This rule guides both judicial and advisory fatwa.
This is a core lesson in rasm al-mufti: knowing when to accept claims of intention.

And if he gives immediate divorce and says 'I intended by it' (the conditional): if a man gives an immediate divorce (without condition) to his wife, then he wants to interpret the words as conditional, so his statement is accepted (diyānah) but not in qaḍā', and the divorce occurs.

And if he says, 'Every woman of mine is divorced' and says 'I intended other than so-and-so', it is not accepted likewise. And in al-Kanz: if a wife says, 'Did you marry over me?' and he says, 'Every woman of mine is divorced,' then the one who made him swear is divorced. And in Sharḥ al-Jāmi‘ by Qāḍī Khān, from Abū Yūsuf that she is not divorced, and our mashāyikh adopted his view. And in al-Mabsūṭ, 'The saying of Abū Yūsuf is more correct in my view.'

Commentary: A man had several wives, he said 'kullu imra'ati li taliq' so all his wives become divorced and his exception intention regarding any wife will not be considered. And in al-Kanz: a wife asked her husband, 'Did you marry someone other than me?' The husband said 'My every wife is divorced.' Then the one who made him swear, since she is also his wife, does divorce occur or not? In this there are two narrations: (1) divorce occurs; (2) the other narration from Imam Abu Yusuf that divorce does not occur, and the mashayikh took this, and Imam Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi in al-Mabsut said it is more correct.

[Arabic:] The divorce of other than her is restricted to it, and Shams al-Aimmah al-Sarakhsi and many mashayikh chose the narration of Abu Yusuf. From Abu Yusuf that she is not divorced because he brought it out as a response, so it applies to her, and because his purpose is to please her. And this is in Jami' Qadi Khan, and our mashayikh adopted it. (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq vol. 2 p. 355)

And if he is asked, 'Do you have a wife other than this woman?' and he says, 'Every woman of mine is divorced,' then this woman is not divorced. The difference between this and the Kanz issue is mentioned in al-Walwajiyyah. And in al-Kanz: 'Every slave of mine is free' – his qin slaves, umm walads, and mudabbars all become free. And in its Sharh by al-Zayla‘i: and if he said 'I intended the males, not the females' – he is believed (diyānah). And likewise if he intended other than the mudabbar. But if he said 'I intended the black ones' or the opposite, he is not believed because the first is specification of the general, and the second is specification of the description, and there is no generality for other than the wording, so the intention of specification does not affect it. And if he intended women instead of men, he is not believed (diyānah either).
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a collection of several rulings on general statements and the role of intention. It opens with the rule: if a husband pronounces an immediate (munjiz) divorce and then claims he intended it to be conditional (ta‘līq), his claim is accepted only between him and Allah (diyānah) but not in court (qaḍā’); the divorce is executed. This is because the literal word is clear, but he may be truthful in his intention. Then it moves to 'kullu imra'ati li taliq': if a man says that, intending to exclude a particular wife, his intention is not accepted either diyānah or qaḍā’ because the general word includes all. Next, a special scenario from Kanz: the wife asks if her husband married another woman, and he responds 'every wife of mine is divorced'. The questioner (muḥallifah, the one who made him swear) – is she divorced? There are two narrations: the first says she is divorced; the second from Abu Yusuf says she is not divorced because the husband's statement is a response to her question, so it applies to her but she is not included? Actually Abu Yusuf argues that since the husband's purpose was to please her or answer her, and he did not intend to divorce her, and because his words were a response, they are limited to her? The passage says 'his intention is restricted to her' (fayataqayyad bihā) meaning he only meant the other wife, not her. The mashayikh prefer Abu Yusuf's view. Then a slightly different case: when someone is asked 'do you have a wife other than this one?' and he responds 'every wife of mine is divorced', then this present wife is not divorced. The difference between this and the Kanz case is explained in al-Walwajiyyah. Finally, an analogous case from manumission: if a man says 'every slave of mine is free', all categories (qin, umm walad, mudabbar) are freed. But if he specifies intention for males only, he is believed diyānah. However, if he specifies by color (black/white) or gender (women/men) when the general word does not have that attribute, his intention is not accepted even diyānan, because this is specification of a description (takhṣīṣ al-waṣf) as opposed to specification of the general (takhṣīṣ al-‘āmm). The principle is that general wording can be specified by intention only if the specification is contained in the general word's scope; here, the general word 'mamlūk' does not imply gender or color for ruling of freedom, so intention cannot restrict it. The lesson emphasizes the limits of niyyah in overriding the literal meaning.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyānatan): Between a person and Allah; the ruling according to the truth of the matter, but not enforceable by a judge.
قضاء (qaḍā’an): In a court of law; the ruling that a judge enforces based on apparent evidence.
محلّفة (muḥallifah): The wife who made her husband swear or prompted him to speak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says 'kullu imra'ati li taliq' and later claims he intended to exclude his wife Zaynab because she was not present at the time. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) His intention is accepted diyānah only, and Zaynab is not divorced.
B) His intention is not accepted at all; Zaynab is also divorced.
C) His intention is accepted in court if he takes an oath.
D) His intention is accepted only if Zaynab agrees.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must weigh the literal meaning of general divorce statements against contextual qarīnah; without strong context, intention does not restrict the general, but when the utterance is a direct response to a question, the response is interpreted according to the question.

Waqf Except for Mosque from Dead Land: Intention Suffices
Exception to waqf rule: mosque from dead land by intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exception where intention alone suffices for a waqf and apply it in a fatwa context.

Core Idea
Intention alone suffices for a mosque from dead land, unlike all other waqf.

This passage refines our understanding of waqf by highlighting a precise exception. While the general rule demands verbal utterance for a valid endowment, the revival of dead land with the specific intention of making it a mosque operates differently. The mufti must recognize this exceptional case to avoid erring in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Recall that in Hanafi fiqh, waqf requires a verbal declaration (talfuz); mere intention is insufficient. This is the default position.
2
Identify the exceptional case
The passage carves out an exception: when a person revives dead land (ard mawat) with the intention of making it a mosque, the land becomes a mosque by that intention alone, no utterance needed.
The authority is Fath al-Qadir, cited by Allamah Hamawi.
3
Understand the legal basis
Reviving dead land grants ownership. When the intention to make it a mosque accompanies revival, the transformation into a mosque is completed by the act and intent together, not by a separate verbal formula.
4
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a person revives a barren plot intending to build a mosque but says nothing. The land becomes a mosque. If they later try to sell it, the sale is invalid.
This contrasts with building a school — that would require a verbal waqf.
5
Note the fatwa implication
A mufti must know this exception. If a questioner asks about a mosque built on revived dead land without utterance, the fatwa should confirm its validity as a mosque.
This is a minor maxim (dabt) that restricts the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها الوقف: دوسرا مسئلہ جو اس قاعدہ سے منتقلی ہے کہ قلب کی نیت کے ہوتے ہوئے تلفظ لا زم ہے وقف کا مسئلہ ہے کہ وقف کے اندر محض دل کی نیت کافی نہ ہوگی بلکہ تلفظ نیت لازم ہے لیکن علامہ حموی نے اس استثنا سے بھی ایک مسئلہ کا استثناء کیا ہے جس میں دل کی نیت کافی ہے کہ کسی شخص نے کسی بنجر جگہ کو مسجد بنانے کی نیت سے آباد کیا تو وہ
محض نیت سے آباد ہو جائے گی تلفظ کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔
اقول و يستثنى من الوقف وقف المسجد لما في فتح القدير اذا حيا ارضا مواتاً بنية جعلها مسجداً كانت مسجداً بمجرد النية ولا يحتاج الى لفظ (حاشيه الموى ص:۸۹)
And among them is waqf: The second issue that is transferred from this rule is that while the heart's intention exists, verbal utterance is necessary—the issue of waqf: in waqf, mere heart's intention is not sufficient; rather, verbal utterance of intention is necessary. But Allamah Hamawi made an exception from this exception to a case where heart's intention suffices: when a person revives a barren land with the intention of making it a mosque, then it becomes [a mosque] by mere intention; there is no need for utterance. I say: And from waqf, the waqf of a mosque is excepted, as in Fath al-Qadir: when he revives dead land with the intention of making it a mosque, it becomes a mosque by mere intention and does not need a verbal expression. (Hashiyat al-Mawi, p. 89)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a second exception to the rule that intention alone suffices: the case of waqf (endowment). Generally, for a valid waqf, verbal utterance is required; mere intention is insufficient. However, Allamah Hamawi (a Hanafi commentator) notes a further exception within this: if a person revives dead land (ard mawat) with the specific intention of making it a mosque, the land becomes a mosque by that intention alone, without any verbal formula. This ruling is sourced from Fath al-Qadir. The mufti should know that for converting dead land into a mosque, no verbal declaration is needed—the intention alone effects the transformation.

Key Terms
وقف (waqf): An endowment of property for a religious or charitable purpose, with the corpus preserved.
ارض موات (ard mawat): Dead land, unused or barren land that can be revived for cultivation or construction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does intention alone suffice for a waqf?
A) For all types of waqf
B) Only for a mosque built on dead land
C) For a waqf of money
D) Intention never suffices for waqf

Key Takeaway
Intention alone transforms dead land into a mosque when revived with that purpose, bypassing the usual waqf utterance requirement.

Clarification: Takbir for Starting Prayer is Not about Intention
Resolving objection: takbir is a condition, not an intention issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the takbir al-ihram as a condition (shart) for commencing prayer and the sufficiency of intention, and apply this distinction to resolve common objections in fatwa.

Core Idea
Takbir al-ihram is a condition for commencing prayer, not an expression of intention.

This brief passage tackles a common point of confusion: if intention (niyyah) in the heart is sufficient for acts of worship, why must a worshipper utter 'Allahu Akbar' to begin prayer? The author clarifies that the takbir is not about voicing the intention; it is an independent condition (shart) for entering the state of prayer. Intention remains an internal act of the heart, while takbir is a verbal condition that marks the transition into the prayer. This distinction is essential for muftis to give correct answers regarding the validity of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that if intention alone suffices, why is verbal takbir required? Recognize that the objector equates takbir with verbalizing intention.
2
Separate intention from conditions
Understand that takbir is not about expressing intention; it is a condition (shart) for initiating prayer. Intention (niyyah) is a separate act of the heart.
3
Apply the distinction
In practice, if a person has intention but does not say takbir, the prayer is invalid because the condition is missing. If a person says takbir but has no intention, the prayer is also invalid. Each requirement stands independently.
4
Formulate a fatwa response
Using this rule, a mufti can answer questions about takbir and intention without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اما توقف شروعه في الصلاة والاحرام: اس جگہ اشکال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ آپ اصل ثانی سے متقی اور مستی منہ کی بات کر رہے ہیں حالانکہ ہم تو یہ دیکھتے ہیں کہ نماز اور احرام کا شروع کرنا بھی تلفظ (ذکر ) پر موقوف ہے اور نیت اس کے لئے کافی نہیں ہوتی اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ نیت سے ان اذکار کا کوئی تعلق نہیں ہے ان کا تعلق نماز اور احرام شروع کرنے سے ہے لہذاوہ شروع کرنے کے شرائط میں سے ہے اشکال کی کوئی وجہ نہیں رہتی۔
وفى وروده تامل اذا الكلام فى التلفظ بالمنوى لابشي آخر.
And as for the commencement of prayer and ihram depending on [verbal expression]: Here an objection arises that you are talking about the second principle and the exception from it, whereas we see that the commencement of prayer and ihram also depends on utterance (dhikr) and intention is not sufficient for it. The answer is that intention has no connection to these utterances; they are related to the commencement of prayer and ihram. Therefore they are among the conditions of commencement; no objection remains. And there is hesitation in its occurrence, for the discussion is about verbalizing what is intended, not about something else.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (tahqiq) addressing a potential objection. The reader might think that the requirement to say 'Allahu Akbar' (takbir) to start prayer contradicts the rule that intention suffices without verbalization. The author clarifies: the takbir is not about expressing the intention; it is a condition (shart) for starting the prayer. Intention (niyyah) is separate—it is the determination in the heart to pray. The takbir is a required act to enter the state of prayer, not a verbalization of intention. Thus there is no contradiction. The mufti must distinguish between conditions of an act and the sufficiency of intention for the act's validity.

Key Terms
تكبيرة الإحرام (takbir al-ihram): The utterance of 'Allahu Akbar' to commence the prayer.
شروع الصلاة (shuru' al-salah): The commencement of the prayer, which requires takbir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does the requirement to say takbir to start prayer contradict the rule that intention suffices without verbalization?
A) Yes, it is a contradiction
B) No, takbir is a condition, not about intention
C) Yes, but only in Ramadan
D) No, because intention is not required at all

Key Takeaway
Takbir al-ihram is a condition for commencing prayer, distinct from intention; they are separate requirements with no contradiction.

The Exception to the Rule: Talaq by Intention in Fatawa Qadhi Khan
Exception to the verbalization requirement for talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi rule that talaq requires verbalization, identify the exceptional case from Fatawa Qadhi Khan, and apply the rule in a fatwa context to distinguish when intention alone suffices.

Core Idea
Talaq normally requires utterance, but in one case from Fatawa Qadhi Khan, intention alone divorces the intended wife.

In Hanafi fiqh, the general principle is that talaq and itq do not take effect by mere intention; they must be verbally pronounced. However, a notable exception is recorded in Fatawa Qadhi Khan. In this lesson, we examine that exceptional case and understand when intention alone can effect a divorce. This distinction is vital for a mufti to avoid incorrect fatwas.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Begin by affirming that talaq and itq require verbal utterance (talfuz). Intention alone does not suffice according to the established Hanafi position.
This is the default ruling found in Zahir al-Riwayah texts.
2
Identify the exceptional case
Read the case from Fatawa Qadhi Khan: a man with two wives, 'Amrah and Zaynab, calls 'O Zaynab!' but 'Amrah responds. He says 'You are divorced thrice.'
The utterance is ambiguous because it is directed at the responder, not the intended wife.
3
Analyze the role of intention
Note that if the man later says 'I intended Zaynab,' then Zaynab is divorced by his mere intention, even though the verbal words were spoken to 'Amrah.
This is a narrow exception where intention alone becomes operative.
4
Understand the scope of the exception
Recognize that this exception is limited to the precise scenario: an ambiguous utterance where the husband clarifies his intent later. It does not permit divorcing by pure unspoken intention in any other case.
A mufti must be careful not to generalize this exception beyond its specific context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الطَّلَاقُ وَالْعِتَاقُ : فَلَا يَقَعَانِ بِالنِّيَّةِ، بَلْ لَا بُدَّ مِنَ التَّلَفُّظِ إِلَّا فِي مَسْأَلَةِ فِي فَتَاوَى قَاضِي خان ، هِيَ رَجُلٌ لَهُ إِمْرَأَتَانِ غَمْرَةٌ ، وَزَيْنَبُ، فَقَالَ : يَا زَيْنَبُ فَأَجَابَتْهُ عُمْرَةً، فَقَالَ: أنتَ طَالِقٌ ثلاثاً، وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ عَلَى الَّتِي أَجَابَتْ إِنْ كَانَتْ إِمْرَأَتُهُ، وَإِنْ لَّمْ تَكُنْ إِمْرَأَتُهُ بَطَلَ لأَنَّهُ أَخْرَجَ الْجَوَابَ جَوَاباً لكلام الَّتِي أَجَابَتْهُ، وَإِنْ قَالَ: نَوَيْتُ زَيْنَبَ طُلَّقَتْ زَيْنَبُ إنتهى فَقَدْ وَقَع الطلاق عَلى زَيْنَبَ بِمُجَرَّدِ النية.

ترجمہ : اور بہر حال طلاق اور عتاق دو نیت سے واقع نہیں ہوتے بلکہ ملنا ضروری ہے مگر فتاوی قاضی خاں کے ایک مسئلہ میں اور وہ یہ ہے تواں نے کہا کہ مجھے تین طلاق تو طلاق اس پر واقع ہو جائے گی جس نے جواب دیا اگر اس کی بیوی ہو اور اگر اس کی ہوئی نہ ہوتو بال ہے اس لئے کہ اس نے اسی کے جواب میں طلاق دی ہے جو اس کی آواز پر بولی تھی اور اگر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے زینب کی نیت کی بھی تو زینب پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی قاضی خاں کا کلام پورا ہوا تو زینب پر حض نیست کہ کسی مرد کی دو بیویاں ہیں عمرہ اور زینب اس نے کہا اے زینب پس عمرہ نے جواب دیا ے خلاق واقع ہوئی۔
As for divorce and manumission: they do not occur by intention; rather, utterance is necessary, except in a case in Fatawa Qadhi Khan: a man has two wives, 'Amrah and Zaynab. He said, 'O Zaynab!' and 'Amrah answered him. He said, 'You are divorced thrice.' The divorce falls on the one who answered, if she is his wife. If she is not his wife, it is void, because he made the response a response to the speech of the one who answered him. And if he said, 'I intended Zaynab,' Zaynab is divorced. End. So divorce occurs on Zaynab by mere intention.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a major exception to the rule that talaq (divorce) and itq (manumission) require verbal utterance. The general principle is that mere intention does not effect a divorce. However, Fatawa Qadhi Khan records a case: a man with two wives, 'Amrah and Zaynab, calls out 'O Zaynab!' and 'Amrah replies. He says 'You are divorced thrice.' The divorce falls on the one who replied if she is his wife, because the utterance was directed at the responder. But if he later says 'I intended Zaynab,' then Zaynab is divorced by his mere intention, even though the words were spoken to 'Amrah. This is a rare case where intention alone causes the divorce. The mufti must know this as a limited exception; generally, talaq requires explicit verbalization.

Key Terms
طلاق (talaq): Dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
فتاوی قاضی خان (Fatawa Qadhi Khan): A collection of fatwas by the Hanafi jurist Qadhi Khan (d. 592/1196).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the husband mistakenly names the wrong wife
B) When the husband says 'You are divorced' without naming anyone
C) When a man calls one wife but another responds, and he later says he intended the first
D) When the wife refuses to speak

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, talaq generally requires verbalization, but a rare exception from Fatawa Qadhi Khan shows that when a man makes an ambiguous statement and later clarifies his intention, the intention alone can effect the divorce on the intended wife.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

8 min
Exception to the Requirement of Utterance in Talaq (Fatawa Qadhi Khan)
Explains a specific exception where mere intention causes talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the exceptional case where intention alone causes talaq and apply the rasm rule to similar ambiguous-addressee scenarios.

Core Idea
In a specific scenario of ambiguous addressee, mere intention determines the effect of talaq, overriding the general rule requiring utterance.

The Hanafi school firmly holds that talaq requires utterance of specific words; mere intention does not effect divorce. However, Fatawa Qadhi Khan records a rare exception: when a husband calls one wife, but another replies, and he uses ambiguous words, his intention decides which wife is divorced. This rasm rule is a critical nuance for muftis handling marital disputes with unclear addresses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Recall that in Hanafi fiqh, talaq requires explicit or implicit words (talaffuz); intention alone is insufficient. This is the default principle.
The passage states: 'طلاق اور عتاق دو نیت سے واقع نہیں ہوتے بلکہ ملنا ضروری ہے' – i.e., talaq and itaq do not occur by intention alone; utterance is necessary.
2
Locate the Exception
Find the exact exception from Fatawa Qadhi Khan: a case where a man says 'I give three divorces' in response to a wife's reply, and his intention determines the recipient.
The exception is explicitly marked: 'مگر فتاوی قاضی خاں کے ایک مسئلہ میں'.
3
Parse the Scenario
Break down the facts: man has two wives (Zainab and 'Amrah). He calls Zainab; 'Amrah responds. He says 'Anti taliq thalathan' (you are thrice divorced). The words are ambiguous as to who is addressed.
The ambiguity arises because the words were uttered after 'Amrah's response, but the intention may have been for Zainab.
4
Apply the Ruling
Determine the outcome: if the husband intended the respondent ('Amrah), talaq falls on her. If he intended the called wife (Zainab), and he states that intention, talaq falls on Zainab. Intention overrides the literal addressee.
The text says: 'اگر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے زینب کی نیت کی بھی تو زینب پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی'.
5
Note the Rasm Rule Implication
Understand that this is an exceptional ruling (istithna) from the general principle. It does not open the door to intention-based talaq in other cases. A mufti must only apply it in the exact scenario of ambiguous addressee.
The passage emphasizes that this is the only issue where mere intention works; elsewhere utterance is mandatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ : اور بہر حال طلاق اور عتاق دو نیت سے واقع نہیں ہوتے بلکہ ملنا ضروری ہے مگر فتاوی قاضی خاں کے ایک مسئلہ میں اور وہ یہ ہے تواں نے کہا کہ مجھے تین طلاق تو طلاق اس پر واقع ہو جائے گی جس نے جواب دیا اگر اس کی بیوی ہو اور اگر اس کی ہوئی نہ ہو تو بال ہے اس لئے کہ اس نے اسی کے جواب میں طلاق دی ہے جو اس کی آواز پر بولی تھی اور اگر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے زینب کی نیت کی بھی تو زینب پر طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی قاضی خاں کا کلام پورا ہوا تو زینب پر حض نیست کہ کسی مرد کی دو بیویاں ہیں عمرہ اور زینب اس نے کہا اے زینب پس عمرہ نے جواب دیا ے خلاق واقع ہوئی۔ تشریح : اس اصل سے مستنی چوتھا مسئلہ ذکر کر رہے ہیں کہ طلاق دو عتاق میں جب تک مخصوص الفاظ نہ ہوں کا کوئی انتہا نہیں ہے۔ و في عرف الفقهاء عبارة عن حكم شرعى يرفع القيد النكاحى بالفاظ مخصوصة (بدايي ج ۳ ص:۳۰) اما ركن الطلاق فهو هذه اللفظة الصادرة من الزوج ( تارتارخانيين ۳ ص: ۲۳۳) إلا في مسئلة في فتاویٰ قاضی خان : اوپر یہ ذکر کیا تھا کہ وقوع طلاق کے لئے تلفظ طلاق ضروری ہے مکن نبت کافی نہیں ہے لیکن ایک خاص مسئلہ کو اس سے مستلخی فرمارہے ہیں کہ ایک شخص کی دو بیویاں ہیں ایک کا نام زیب ہے اور ایک کا نام عمرہ ہے اس نے زینب کو آواز دی عمرہ نے اس کی آواز پر ہاں کہا اس نے کہا انت طالق ثلاثاء ی تین طلاقیں جواب دینے والی پر پڑیں گی کیونکہ الفاظ کا تلفظ عمرہ پر واقع ہوا ہے کیونکہ نفس الامر میں وہی مخاطب ہے اور اگر جواب دینے والی اس کی بیوی نہ ہو تو پھر طلاق اس کی بیوی پر واقع نہ ہوگی اور نہ ہی اس عورت پر ہوگی ،عمرہ نے آواز پر جواب دیا اور شوہر کہتا ہے کہ میری نیت زینب کو طلاق دینے کی تھی تو اس صورت میں محض نیت کا اعتبار کے زینب پر طلاق پڑ جائے گی لہذا یہی وہ مسئلہ ہے جس میں محض نیت سے بھی طلاق پڑ جاتی ہے۔
Translation: In any case, divorce and manumission do not occur by intention alone; utterance is necessary, except in one issue in Fatawa Qadhi Khan, which is that a man said, 'May I give three divorces?' then divorce will fall on the one who replied if she is his wife, and if she is not his wife then no effect, because he gave divorce in response to her answer. If he said 'I intended Zainab,' then divorce falls on Zainab. Qadhi Khan's speech ends. Explanation: The fourth exception from this principle is being mentioned: in divorce and manumission, unless specific words are used, there is no effect. And in the terminology of jurists, it is a legal ruling that removes the marital tie with specific words. As for the pillar of divorce, it is the utterance from the husband. Except in an issue in Fatawa Qadhi Khan: it was mentioned above that uttering divorce is necessary for its occurrence; mere intention is insufficient. But a special case is being exempted: a man has two wives, one named Zainab and one named 'Amrah. He called Zainab; 'Amrah responded. He said, 'Anti taliq thalathan' (you are thrice divorced). The three divorces fall on the respondent because the words were uttered to 'Amrah, as she was the actual addressee. If the respondent is not his wife, then divorce falls neither on his wife nor on that woman. But if the husband says 'I intended Zainab,' then in that case mere intention is considered and divorce falls on Zainab. This is the issue where divorce occurs by mere intention.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage discusses a rasm rule: talaq and itaq require explicit utterance of words; mere intention is insufficient. However, it presents a specific exception from Fatawa Qadhi Khan. The ruling: If a man calls one of his wives by name, but the other wife responds, and he says 'Anti taliq,' the divorce falls on the respondent (the one who answered) because the words are directed at her. But his intention matters: if he intended the first wife (the one he called), then divorce falls on that one. Thus, intention alone can determine the recipient of talaq in this scenario. The passage explains that this is an exception to the rule that intention alone does not cause talaq. The exception is limited to this case where the husband spoke ambiguous words or directed them in response to a reply. The Hanafi position is that ordinarily talaq requires specific wording addressed to the wife. However, here due to the ambiguity of addressee, intention becomes decisive.

Key Terms
نیت (niyyah): Intention; in this context, it refers to the husband's inner resolve to divorce a specific wife when uttering ambiguous words.
تلفظ (talaffuz): Utterance of divorce words; a necessary condition for talaq except in this exceptional case.
فتاوی قاضی خان (Fatawa Qadhi Khan): A collection of fatwas by Qadhi Khan, a Hanafi jurist; this work is considered reliable for later fatwa.

A) On wife B because she replied
B) On wife A because of his intention
C) Neither because intention alone doesn't work
D) Both wives

Key Takeaway
Mere intention can cause talaq only in the specific ambiguous-addressee scenario; otherwise, utterance is required.

Non-Muslim's Intention: Principle and Exception for Qasr
Explaining the rule that a non-Muslim's intention is invalid with its exception in qasr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that a non-Muslim's intention (niyyah) is invalid and identify the exception where his mere intention (qasd) is considered for qasr prayer after accepting Islam.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim's niyyah is legally irrelevant, but his qasd counts for qasr — intention without devotion still matters in travel.

In Hanafi fiqh, intention (niyyah) is a condition for acts of worship. Since a non-Muslim lacks the foundational belief, their spiritual intention is not recognized. However, a key exception appears in the rule of qasr (shortening prayer) during travel: if a non-Muslim sets out on a journey and accepts Islam en route, his original intent (qasd) to travel is considered, allowing him to shorten prayer. This lesson unpacks that principle and its exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
What is the default ruling regarding a non-Muslim's intention (niyyah) for worship? General: invalid.
2
Locate the exception
In what specific scenario is the non-Muslim's intention given weight? When he accepts Islam during a three-day journey and then shortens prayer.
3
Understand the distinction
Why niyyah vs qasd? Niyyah requires religious devotion; qasd is mere purpose. The exception uses qasd, not niyyah.
4
Contrast with the child
Why does the boy who reaches puberty mid-journey not shorten? Because his prior journey was not legally binding (he was not responsible).
5
Extract the principle for fatwa
When issuing a fatwa, the mufta bihi is that the non-Muslim's qasd is considered in qasr, but not for other acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فِي الْبَزَّازِيَّةِ وَالْخُلَاصَةِ : هِيَ صَبِيٌّ وَنَصْرَانِيٌّ خَرَجَا إِلَىٰ مَسِيْرَةِ ثَلَاثٍ، فَبَلَغَ الصَّبِيُّ فِي بَعْضِ الطَّرِيقِ وَأَسْلَمَ الْكَافِرُ قَصَّرَ الْكَافِرُ لِاعْتِبَارِ قَصْدِهِ لَا الصَّبِيُّ فِي الْمُخْتَارِ، إِنْتَهى.
والنصراني اذا تعلم القرآن يعلم والفقه كذلك لانه عسى يهتدى.
المصحف مطلقاً. وإذا اغتسل ثم مس لا بأس به فى قول محمد و عنهما يمنع من مس.
ولا كفارة يمين كافر و إن حنث مسلما بأية لانهم لا ايمان لهم و اما ان نكثوا ايمانهم فيعنى الصورة كتحليف الحاكم.
بخلاف كافر اسلم فانه يقصر.
و قد بقى لمقصده اقل من ثلاثة ايام فانه يتم ولا يعتبر ما مضى لعدم تكليفه فيه.
In al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa: It is a boy and a Christian who set out for a journey of three days, then the boy reached puberty on some part of the road and the disbeliever accepted Islam; the disbeliever shortens prayer due to consideration of his intention, not the boy, in the chosen view. End. And the Christian, when he learns the Quran and fiqh likewise, because perhaps he may be guided. The mushaf absolutely. And if he bathes then touches, there is no harm in the view of Muhammad, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is prevented from touching. And there is no expiation for the oath of a disbeliever, even if he breaks it as a Muslim because they have no faith. And 'if they break their oaths' means figuratively, like the judge's swearing. Contrary to a disbeliever who becomes Muslim, for he shortens. And for the one who has less than three days remaining to his destination, he completes and does not consider what has passed due to lack of obligation upon him.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal principle that a non-Muslim's intention (niyyah) is generally not considered in Islamic law, with an exception in the case of qasr (shortening prayer) when he accepts Islam mid-journey. The author presents several applications: teaching a non-Muslim the Quran and fiqh is permissible in hope of guidance; touching the mushaf is absolutely prohibited for a non-Muslim, but after ghusl Imam Muhammad allows it while the two shaykhs prohibit; the oath of a non-Muslim is not valid and no kaffarah is due, even if he breaks it after becoming Muslim, referencing the Quranic verse 'and if they break their oaths' interpreted figuratively. The exception comes in the qasr case: if a non-Muslim sets out on a journey of three days and accepts Islam en route, he is allowed to shorten prayer based on his original intention (qasd), distinguishing between mere intent and religious intention. The child who reaches puberty en route does not shorten because his prior journey was not legally valid.

Key Terms
نِیَّت (niyyah): Intention directed towards worship or legal acts; for a non-Muslim, generally not considered valid.
قَصْد (qasd): Mere intention or purpose without religious devotion; distinguished from niyyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view (mukhtar), if a non-Muslim sets out on a three-day journey and accepts Islam during the journey, does he shorten his prayer?
A) Yes, because his original intention (qasd) is considered.
B) No, because he was not a Muslim at the start.
C) Yes, but only if he had bathed.
D) No, because he must complete the prayer as a traveler.

Key Takeaway
A non-Muslim's niyyah is invalid, but his qasd is considered for qasr when he converts mid-journey.
