Non-Muslim's Interaction with Islamic Texts and Oaths
Explaining applications of the rule: teaching, mushaf touching, oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule regarding a non-Muslim's intention to the specific cases of teaching Quran/fiqh, touching the mushaf, and oath expiation, including the intra-madhhab dispute on mushaf touching.

Core Idea
Teaching a non-Muslim the Quran is allowed; touching the mushaf contested; his oath has no expiation.

Beyond the general rule on intention, the passage from al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa provides practical rulings on a non-Muslim's interaction with Islamic texts and oaths. Teaching him the Quran and fiqh is permissible in hope of guidance. Touching the mushaf is absolutely prohibited in the preferred view, though Imam Muhammad allowed it after ghusl. The oath of a non-Muslim carries no expiation even if broken after Islam, because their oath was not based on faith. This lesson unpacks these applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rationale for teaching
Why is teaching a non-Muslim the Quran allowed? Because he might be guided.
2
Analyze the mushaf touching dispute
Locate the two positions: Imam Muhammad allows after ghusl; the two shaykhs forbid absolutely.
3
Determine the mufta bihi
The preferred view is absolute prohibition (man' mutlaq) per the two shaykhs.
4
Understand the oath ruling
Why no kaffarah? Because the non-Muslim has no iman to make the oath binding; the Quranic verse about breaking oaths applies figuratively.
5
Contrast with the convert's oath
If he breaks the oath after Islam, still no expiation because the oath was taken in kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فِي الْبَزَّازِيَّةِ وَالْخُلَاصَةِ : هِيَ صَبِيٌّ وَنَصْرَانِيٌّ خَرَجَا إِلَىٰ مَسِيْرَةِ ثَلَاثٍ، فَبَلَغَ الصَّبِيُّ فِي بَعْضِ الطَّرِيقِ وَأَسْلَمَ الْكَافِرُ قَصَّرَ الْكَافِرُ لِاعْتِبَارِ قَصْدِهِ لَا الصَّبِيُّ فِي الْمُخْتَارِ، إِنْتَهى.
والنصراني اذا تعلم القرآن يعلم والفقه كذلك لانه عسى يهتدى.
المصحف مطلقاً. وإذا اغتسل ثم مس لا بأس به فى قول محمد و عنهما يمنع من مس.
ولا كفارة يمين كافر و إن حنث مسلما بأية لانهم لا ايمان لهم و اما ان نكثوا ايمانهم فيعنى الصورة كتحليف الحاكم.
بخلاف كافر اسلم فانه يقصر.
و قد بقى لمقصده اقل من ثلاثة ايام فانه يتم ولا يعتبر ما مضى لعدم تكليفه فيه.
In al-Bazzaziyya and al-Khulasa: It is a boy and a Christian who set out for a journey of three days, then the boy reached puberty on some part of the road and the disbeliever accepted Islam; the disbeliever shortens prayer due to consideration of his intention, not the boy, in the chosen view. End. And the Christian, when he learns the Quran and fiqh likewise, because perhaps he may be guided. The mushaf absolutely. And if he bathes then touches, there is no harm in the view of Muhammad, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he is prevented from touching. And there is no expiation for the oath of a disbeliever, even if he breaks it as a Muslim because they have no faith. And 'if they break their oaths' means figuratively, like the judge's swearing. Contrary to a disbeliever who becomes Muslim, for he shortens. And for the one who has less than three days remaining to his destination, he completes and does not consider what has passed due to lack of obligation upon him.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal principle that a non-Muslim's intention (niyyah) is generally not considered in Islamic law, with an exception in the case of qasr (shortening prayer) when he accepts Islam mid-journey. The author presents several applications: teaching a non-Muslim the Quran and fiqh is permissible in hope of guidance; touching the mushaf is absolutely prohibited for a non-Muslim, but after ghusl Imam Muhammad allows it while the two shaykhs prohibit; the oath of a non-Muslim is not valid and no kaffarah is due, even if he breaks it after becoming Muslim, referencing the Quranic verse 'and if they break their oaths' interpreted figuratively. The exception comes in the qasr case: if a non-Muslim sets out on a journey of three days and accepts Islam en route, he is allowed to shorten prayer based on his original intention (qasd), distinguishing between mere intent and religious intention. The child who reaches puberty en route does not shorten because his prior journey was not legally valid.

Key Terms
نِیَّت (niyyah): Intention directed towards worship or legal acts; for a non-Muslim, generally not considered valid.
قَصْد (qasd): Mere intention or purpose without religious devotion; distinguished from niyyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) True, because ghusl removes impurity.
B) False, because the prohibition is absolute.
C) True, but only if he intends to learn.
D) False, because he must make wudu as well.

Key Takeaway
Teaching non-Muslims is allowed; touching the mushaf is absolutely prohibited; their oaths carry no expiation.

Mere Intention Does Not Invalidate Acts of Worship
Explaining that mere intention does not break worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that mere intention (niyyah) without action does not break an act of worship, and distinguish between prayer (different genres) and fasting (same genre) in this context.

Core Idea
Mere intention to break worship does nothing; only actual contradictory action invalidates.

This lesson examines a key rule from Hanafi rasm al-mufti: the effect of intention on ongoing acts of worship. The text clarifies that simply intending to cut off prayer or fasting does not invalidate them. The analysis delves into examples from prayer, fasting, and zakat, and explains the critical distinction between genres (jins) in worship types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Rule
Read the opening statement: 'ولو نوى قطع الصلاة' and the surrounding explanation. The rule state that mere intention does not cut off practical acts of worship (عملی عبادات).
2
Analyze the Prayer Example
Consider the example: someone starts Zuhr prayer, then intends Asr prayer without any action. The passage says the Zuhr prayer remains valid until an actual contradictory action is performed (e.g., another takbir). Understand why mere intention is insufficient.
The criterion (مناط) for exiting the first prayer is the validity of starting something different, even if in some respect.
3
Examine the Fasting Example
Review the case of obligatory fasting after dawn: intending to break it or switch to nafl does not invalidate the fast. Contrast this with the prayer example. Identify the key distinction: in prayer, fard and nafl are different genres (جنسان مختلفان); in fasting, they are one genre (جنس واحد).
In prayer, the takbirat al-ihram cannot be shared between fard and nafl, so mere intention cannot transfer genres.
4
Reconcile the Apparent Contradiction from Khizanat al-Akmal
The text quotes Khizanat al-Akmal saying that changing intention from fard to nafl within prayer makes it nafl. This seems to contradict the earlier statement. Understand the resolution: the fard prayer is lost and replaced by nafl, but the ruling is not that the fard continues. This illustrates a nuanced application.
The author suggests that the change is effective in a partial sense (من وجہ): the fard is nullified but the nafl is attained.
5
Apply the Rule to New Scenarios
Use the rule to predict outcomes: e.g., someone in prayer intends to eat later (no action), or someone fasting intends to break at sunset (before any action). The rule states these intentions have no effect on validity.
Also note the exception: retracting intention for fasting before dawn is valid, as per the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۵۲
ولو نوى قطع الصلاة : يہ مسئلہ پیچھے بھی گزر چکا ہے کہ عملی عبادات شخص کیسے سے منقطع نہیں ہوتیں ہیں اور نہ ی باطل ہوتی ہیں، لہذا کسی نے ظہر کی نیت سے تکبیر کہی پھر عصر کی نماز کی نیت کر لی تو جب تک وہ منافی صلاۃ مل کر کے دوبارہ تکبیر نہ کہے گا سابقہ نماز محض نیت سے منقطع نہ ہوگی۔
فمناط الخروج عن الاولى صحة الشروع في المغاير ولو من وجه (البحر ١٦:٠٣) وَأَمَّا صَوْمُ الْفَرْضِ إِذَا شَرعَ فِيهِ بَعْدَ الْفَجْرِ، ثُمَّ نَوَى قَطْعَهُ، وَالانْتِقَالَ إِلَى صَوْمِ النَّفْلِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يَبْطُلُ، وَالْفَرْقُ أَنَّ الْفَرْضَ وَالنَّفْلَ فِي الصَّلَاةِ جِنْسَانِ مُخْتَلِفَانِ لَا رُجْحَانَ لأَحَدِهِمَا عَلَى الْآخَرِفِي التَّحْرِيْمَةِ، وَهُمَا فِي الصَّوْمِ وَالزَّكَاةِ جِنْسٌ وَاحِدٌ، كَذَا فِي الْمُحِيْطِ». وَفِي خِزَانَةِ الأَكْمَلِ: لَوِ افْتَتَحَ الصَّلَاةَ بِنِيَّةِ الْفَرْضِ ثُمَّ غَيْرَ نِيَّتَهُ فِي الصَّلاةِ، وَجَعَلَهَا تَطَوُّعاً صَارَتْ تَطَوُّعاً، وَلَوْ نَوَى الْأَكْلَ أَوِ الْجِمَاعَ فِي الصَّوْمِ لَا يَضُرُّهُ، وَكَذَا لَوْنَوَى فِعْلَا مُنَافِياً فِى الصَّلَاةِ لَمْ تَبْطُلْ. ترجمه اور فرض روز و جب فجر کے بعد شروع کر دیا پھر سے ختم کر سے نفل روزہ کی طرف منتقل ہونے کی نیت کر لی تو وہ باطل نہیں ہوگا اور فرق یہ ہے کہ فرض و نفل نماز میں دو مختلف اجناس میں تحریمہ کے اعتبار سے ان میں سے ایک کو دوسرے پر کوئی ترجیح حاصل نہیں ہے اور روزہ وزکوۃ میں وہ دونوں ایک ہی جنس ہیں محیط میں اسی طرح مذکور ہے۔ اور خزانة الأكمل میں ہے کہ اگر کسی نے فرض کی نیت سے نماز شروع کی پھر دوران نماز ہی اپنی نیت بدل لی اور اسے نفل بنا دیا تو وہ نفل ہو جائے گی اور اگر روزہ میں کھانے یا جماع کی نیت کر لی تو وہ مضر نہیں ہے اور ایسے ہی اگر نماز میں کسی منافی صلوۃ کی نیت کر لی تو بھی مضر نہیں۔ تشریح اگر کسی شخص نے فرض روزہ رکھا پھر فجر کے بعد اسے ختم کر کے نفل روزہ کی نیت کر لی تو اس کی نیت درست نہ ہوگی اور فرض روزہ باطل نہ ہو گا۔
والفرق ان الفرض والنفل: صاحب الشیاء علامہ ابن نجیم المصری فرماتے ہیں کہ نماز میں فرض اور نفل دو مختلف جنس ہیں تحریمہ کے اعتبار سے ایک کو دوسرے پر کوئی ترجیح نہیں جبکہ صوم و زکوۃ میں یہ ایک ہی جنس شمار ہوتے ہیں یعنی نماز میں تکبیر تحریمہ کے ذریعہ فرض سے نفل کی طرف انتقال ممکن ہے لیکن روزہ اور زکوۃ میں انتقال کے لئے کوئی مستقل عمل نہیں ہے۔ و في خزانة الأكمل : یہاں اشکال یہ ہوتا ہے کہ چند سطریں اوپر کہا تھا کہ محض نیت سے ایک عبادت ہے اس جنس کی دوسری عبادت کی طرف منتقل نہیں ہوا جا سکتا ہے اور یہاں اس کے خلاف کہہ رہے ہیں ممکن ہے کہ مصنف یہ کہنا چاہ رہے ہوں کہ اس طرح نیت کرنے سے اس کی فرض نماز من وجہ نفل ہو جائے یعنی اس کی فرض نماز تو الیا ہو جائے گی لیکن اسے تو اب نفل کا ملے گا۔ واللہ سبحانہ و تعالیٰ اعلم
ولو نوى الاكل او الجماع : اگر کسی نے دوران روزہ کھانے پینے یا جماع کی نیت کی تو اس نیت سے اس کے روزہ میں کچھ فرق نہیں پڑے گا۔
اذ نوى الصائم الفطر ولم يحدث شيئا غير النية فصومه تام (بدائع : ۱۹۵) اگر کسی نے رات میں روزہ کی نیت کی تھی لیکن صبح صادق ہونے سے پہلے پہلے نیت بدل دی تو اس کی نیت کا علم ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
ولونوى من الليل ثم رجع عن نيته قبل طلوع الفجر صح رجوعه في الصيامات كلها.
(هندیه ج ا ص ۱۹۵) 
و كذا لونوى فعلا منافياً فی الصلوۃ: اگر کسی نے فرض نماز ظہر کی شروع کی اور دوران نماز عصر کی نماز کی نیت کر لی یا کسی منافی عمل کی نیت کی تو اس کی نیت کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا اور اس کی شروع کردہ نماز باطل نہ ہو گی۔
And if he intended to cut off prayer: This issue has passed before that practical acts of worship are not cut off by mere intention, nor are they invalidated. So if someone said takbir with the intention of Zuhr, then intended the prayer of Asr, as long as he does not do something contradictory to prayer and say takbir again, the previous prayer will not be cut off by mere intention. The criterion for exiting the first (prayer) is the validity of starting something different, even if in some respect (al-Bahr 16:03). And as for obligatory fasting, when one begins it after dawn, then intends to cut it off and transition to voluntary fasting, it does not become invalid. The difference is that fard and nafl in prayer are two different genres; neither has preference over the other in the takbirat al-ihram, while in fasting and zakat they are one genre. So it is in al-Muhit. And in Khizanat al-Akmal: If one opens prayer with the intention of fard, then changes his intention within the prayer and makes it nafl, it becomes nafl. And if he intended eating or intercourse in fasting, it does not harm him. Similarly, if he intended a contradictory act in prayer, it does not become invalid. Translation: And obligatory fasting: when he starts after dawn, then intends to end it and transition to voluntary fasting, it does not become invalid. The difference is that fard and nafl in prayer are two different genres in terms of the takbirat al-ihram; neither has preference over the other, while in fasting and zakat they are one genre. So it is mentioned in al-Muhit. And in Khizanat al-Akmal: If someone started prayer with the intention of fard, then changed his intention during the prayer and made it nafl, it becomes nafl. And if he intended eating or intercourse in fasting, it is not harmful. Similarly, if he intended a contradictory act in prayer, it is not harmful. Explanation: If someone kept an obligatory fast, then after dawn intended to end it and start a voluntary fast, his intention is not valid and the obligatory fast does not become invalid. The difference between fard and nafl: The author, Ibn Nujaym al-Misri, says that in prayer, fard and nafl are different genres; in terms of the takbirat al-ihram, neither has preference over the other, while in fasting and zakat they are considered one genre. That is, in prayer, transition from fard to nafl is possible through the takbirat al-ihram, but in fasting and zakat there is no independent action for transition. And in Khizanat al-Akmal: Here an objection arises: a few lines above it was said that one cannot transition from one act of worship to another of a different genre by mere intention, and here they are saying the opposite. It is possible that the author means that by such intention, his fard prayer becomes nafl in some respect, i.e., his fard prayer is lost, but he will get the reward of nafl. And Allah knows best. And if he intended eating or intercourse: If someone during fasting intended eating, drinking, or intercourse, this intention does not affect his fast. When the fasting person intends to break the fast and does not do anything other than the intention, his fast is complete (Bada'i': 195). If someone intended fasting at night, then changed his intention before true dawn, his intention becomes void. And if he intended at night then retracted his intention before dawn, his retraction is valid in all fasts. (Hindiyya vol. 1 p. 195) And similarly, if he intended a contradictory act in prayer: If someone started the fard prayer of Zuhr and during the prayer intended the prayer of Asr, or intended a contradictory action, his intention is not considered and his started prayer does not become invalid.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule governing the effect of intention (niyyah) on acts of worship. The rule is: mere intention to cut off or change an act of worship does not invalidate it. The author supports this with examples from prayer and fasting. In prayer, if one starts Zuhr and then intends Asr without actually doing anything contradictory, the Zuhr prayer remains valid. The same applies in fasting: intending to eat or have intercourse does not break the fast. The text then explains the difference between prayer and fasting: in prayer, fard and nafl are distinct genres (jins) and neither has preference over the other in the takbirat al-ihram; thus, one cannot transfer from fard to nafl by mere intention. In fasting and zakat, fard and nafl are one genre, so the intention does not break the original obligation. A seeming contradiction arises from Khizanat al-Akmal, which states that changing intention from fard to nafl within prayer makes it nafl. The author reconciles by suggesting that the fard prayer is lost and replaced by nafl, but the ruling is not that the fard prayer continues. The passage also covers the case of retracting intention before dawn in fasting, which is valid. All examples reinforce the rule that mere intention without action does not nullify worship.

Key Terms
نیة (niyyah): Intention; the mental resolve to perform a specific act of worship.
جنس (jins): Genre or category; used here to distinguish between fard and nafl in prayer (different genres) versus fasting and zakat (same genre).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a person starts praying Zuhr and then intends Asr without doing anything else?
A) The Zuhr prayer becomes invalid.
B) The Zuhr prayer remains valid.
C) The prayer becomes Asr automatically.
D) The Zuhr prayer becomes nafl.

Key Takeaway
Mere intention to break or change an act of worship has no effect; only actual actions invalidate or modify worship.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
The Five Conditions for a Traveler’s Intention of Residence (Iqamah)
Conditions for valid iqamah intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions for a traveler's iqamah to be valid and identify which condition is violated in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Five conditions must be met — ceasing movement, suitable place, single location, duration, and independence — not just intention alone.

A traveler does not automatically become a resident simply by intending to stay. The Hanafi school specifies five conditions that must all be fulfilled. This lesson unpacks each condition and also addresses a special case: what happens if the intention occurs during prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a traveler intending to end his travel and establish residence (iqamah). If the conditions are met, he becomes a resident (muqim) and travel concessions (e.g., qasr prayer) end.
2
Enumerate the five conditions
Recall the five conditions: (1) ceasing movement, (2) suitable place, (3) unity of place, (4) duration, (5) independence of opinion. These must all be present simultaneously.
3
Explain each condition briefly
Condition 1: must stop traveling; intending while walking is invalid. Condition 2: the location must be land fit for settlement, not sea or uninhabited island. Condition 3: must intend a single spot, not multiple locations. Condition 4: a specific duration is required (classically 15 days or more, though the text does not specify the number here). Condition 5: the person must be independent in decision; a follower (e.g., wife, child) cannot validly intend iqamah.
The duration condition is elaborated elsewhere in Hanafi texts as a minimum of 15 days.
4
Apply the conditions to a scenario
Scenario: A traveler is still on a bus when he thinks, 'I will stay in this city for 10 days.' He does not get off the bus. Is he a resident? No, because condition 1 (ceasing movement) is not met. He must physically stop traveling for the intention to take effect.
5
Note the subsidiary ruling on intention during prayer
If a traveler intends iqamah while performing a prayer (within the prayer time), his obligation changes from two rak'ahs to four, regardless of when in the prayer he makes the intention, and regardless of being imam, follower, or alone.
This shows the power of intention to alter the prayer mid-act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْنَوَى قَطعَ السَّفْرِ بِالْإِقَامَةِ صَارَ مُقِيمًا، وَبَطَلَ سَفَرُهُ بِخَمْسِ شَرَائِطَ: تَرْكَ السَّيْرِ، حَتَّى لَوْ نَوَى الْإِقَامَةَ سَائِرًا لَمْ تَصِحُ، وَصَلَاحِيَةُ الْمَوْضَعِ لِلْإِقَامَةِ فَلَوْ نَوَاهَا فِي بَحْرٍ أَوْ جَزِيْرَةٍ لَمْ تَصِحُ، وَإِتِّحَادُ الْمَوْضَعِ، وَالْمُدَّةُ، وَالْإِسْتِقْلَالُ بِالرَّأْيِ فَلَا تَصِحُ نِيَّةُ التَّابِعِ، كَذَا فِي مِعْرَاجِ الدَّرَايَةِ. وَإِذَا نَوَى الْمُسَافِرُ الْإِقَامَةَ فِي إِثْنَاءِ صَلَاتِهِ فِي الْوَقْتِ، تَحَوَّلَ فَرْضُهُ إِلَى الْأَرْبَعِ سَوَاءٌ نَوَاهَا فِي أَوَّلِهَا، أَوْ فِي أَوْسَطِهَا، أَوْ فِي آخِرِهَا وَسَوَاءٌ كَانَ مُنْفَرِدًا، أَوْ مُقْتَدِيًا، أَوْ مُدْرِكًا، أَوْ مَسْبُوقًا. ترجمه: اور اگر سفر ختم کر کے اقامت کی نیت کر لی تو وہ مقیم ہو جائے گا اور پانچ شرائط کے ساتھ اس کا سفر باطل ہو جائے گا: چلنا چھوڑ نا، اور اگر چلتے چلتے اقامت کی نیت کی تو وہ درست نہیں، موضع اقامت کا اقامت کے لائق ہوتا اگر کسی سمندر یا جزیرہ پر اس (اقامت) کی نیت کی تو وہ درست نہیں، موضع اقامت کا ایک ہوتا، اور مدت، اور مستقل بالرائی ہو نا چنانچہ تابع کی نیت درست نہیں معراج الدرایہ میں اسی طرح ہے۔ اور اگر مسافر نے وقت کے اندر اپنی نماز کے دوران اقامت کی نیت کر لی تو اس کا فرض چار سے بدل جائے گا خواہ اس نے اس (اقامت) کی نیت اس (نماز) کے شروع میں کی ہو یا اس کے درمیان یا اس کے آخر میں کی ہو اور برابر ہے کہ وہ منفرد ہو یا مقتدی یا مدرک یا مسبوق۔ اگر مسافر نے دوران سفر اقامت کی نیت کر لی تو اقامت کی نیت سے وہ مقیم ہو جائے گا اور اس کا سفر شرعی باطل ہو جائے گا مگر پانچ شرطوں کے ساتھ: (۱) چلنا بند کر دے حتی کہ اگر چلتے ہوئے اقامت کی نیت کی تو وہ درست نہ ہوگا اور ترک سفر سے مراد کسی بستی میں ٹھہر
And if he intended to cut off travel by establishing residence, he becomes a resident, and his travel becomes void with five conditions: (1) ceasing movement, so if he intended residence while traveling, it is not valid; (2) the place being suitable for residence, so if he intended it at sea or an island, it is not valid; (3) unity of place; (4) the duration; (5) independence of opinion, so the intention of a follower is not valid. So it is in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. And if the traveler intends residence during his prayer within the time, his obligation changes to four (rak'ahs), whether he intended it at the beginning, middle, or end of it, and whether he is praying alone, as a follower, as one who caught the prayer, or as one who missed part. Translation: And if he intends to end travel and establish residence, he becomes a resident, and his travel becomes void with five conditions: ceasing movement—if he intends residence while walking, it is not valid; the place of residence must be suitable for residence—if he intends it at sea or an island, it is not valid; unity of place; duration; and independence of opinion—thus the intention of a follower is not valid. So it is in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. And if the traveler intends residence during his prayer within the time, his obligation changes to four, whether he intended it at the beginning, middle, or end of it, and whether he is alone, a follower, one who caught the prayer, or one who is preceded. If a traveler intends residence during travel, he becomes a resident by that intention, and his travel becomes void according to Shariah, but with five conditions: (1) he must stop moving—even if he intends residence while walking, it is not valid, and 'ceasing travel' means stopping in a town...
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule detailing the conditions under which a traveler's intention to establish residence (iqamah) ends the legal status of travel. The rule states that if a traveler intends to end his travel and reside, he becomes a resident, but only if five conditions are fulfilled: (1) he must stop traveling—intending while still moving is insufficient; (2) the place must be suitable for residence (e.g., not on a ship or uninhabited island); (3) the place of residence must be a single location (not multiple); (4) the duration of intended stay must be specified (classically, 15 days or more, though not mentioned here); (5) he must be independent in decision-making—the intention of a dependent (e.g., a wife or child) is not valid. The source cited is Mi'raj al-Dirayah. Additionally, if a traveler intends residence during a prayer that he is performing, his obligation changes from two rak'ahs (qasr) to four rak'ahs, regardless of when in the prayer he makes the intention, and regardless of his position as imam, follower, etc. This shows that intention can affect prayer length even mid-prayer.

Key Terms
اقامة (iqamah): The intention to settle in a place, ending the legal status of travel (safar).
شرائط (shara'it): Conditions that must be met for the intention of iqamah to be legally effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five conditions for a traveler's intention of iqamah to be valid?
A) Ceasing movement
B) The place being suitable for residence
C) The intention being made in prayer
D) Independence of opinion

Key Takeaway
A traveler's intention to become a resident is only effective if five conditions are simultaneously satisfied: ceasing travel, a suitable single location, a specified duration, and independent decision-making.

The Effect of Mere Intention to Break the Fast
Ruling that mere intention does not invalidate fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that mere intention to break the fast does not invalidate the fast and apply the fatwa hierarchy principle that intentions are judged by external actions.

Core Idea
Mere intention does not break the fast – only action invalidates.

A common question among laypeople and scholars alike is whether merely thinking about breaking the fast invalidates it. This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair provides a clear rule: as long as no action follows, the fast remains complete. The ruling rests on the fundamental legal maxim that intentions without external manifestation have no legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and scenario
The passage describes a fasting person who intends to break the fast but does nothing else. Recognize that the act is purely internal.
No eating, drinking, or intercourse occurs.
2
Determine the legal ruling
The ruling is that the fast remains valid (tamām). The intention alone does not nullify.
This is the muftā bihī position.
3
Identify the operative principle
The underlying principle is that intentions without accompanying actions do not affect the validity of worship. Only actual nullifiers (eating, drinking, intercourse) break the fast.
Contrast with the requirement of intention at the start of the fast.
4
Recognize the legal category
This is a rasm al-mufti rule — a clear, unequivocal ruling for a mufti to apply without dispute.
It is derived from the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' but with a specific application.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
The ruling is from the later Hanafi works (fatawa mutakhkhirin) and is relied upon in fatwa. Note the absence of ikhtilaf.
Epistemological weight: mufta bihi without conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو نوى الصائم الفطر ولم يحدث شيئا غير النية فصومه تام
And if a fasting person intends to break the fast and does not produce anything other than the intention, his fast is complete.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: mere intention to break the fast, without any accompanying action, does not invalidate the fast. The fast remains valid (tamām). This is based on the principle that intentions alone, without external manifestation, do not affect the validity of an act of worship. The Hanafi school holds that the fast is broken only by actual consumption or sexual intercourse, not by a mere resolve. This ruling applies to obligatory Ramadan fasts as well as voluntary fasts.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): intention in the heart

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does a fasting person's mere intention to break his fast without any action invalidate his fast according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, it invalidates the fast
B) No, the fast remains valid
C) It invalidates only if the intention is firm
D) It invalidates only after sunset

Key Takeaway
Mere intention to break the fast does not invalidate the fast; only action breaks it.

Retraction of Fast Intention Before Dawn
Ruling on retraction of intention before dawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that retraction of intention before dawn is valid for all types of fasts, and distinguish between voluntary and obligatory fasts regarding the timing of intention.

Core Idea
Retracting your intention to fast before dawn cancels the obligation in all fasts.

This lesson examines a rasm al-mufti rule regarding the validity of retracting one's intention to fast before dawn. The rule confirms that such retraction is effective, freeing the individual from the obligation to fast. This principle is essential for muftis advising on niyyah (intention) in voluntary and obligatory fasts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of fast
Determine whether the fast is obligatory (e.g., Ramadan, qada) or voluntary (nafl). The rule applies to all, but voluntary fasts have broader flexibility.
2
Check the timing of retraction
Ensure the retraction occurs before the break of dawn (fajr). After dawn, the intention is fixed and retraction is invalid.
3
Apply the ruling
State that the retraction is valid, so the person is no longer required to fast. If it is a voluntary fast, they may choose to fast later or not at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولونوى من الليل ثم رجع عن نيته قبل طلوع الفجر صح رجوعه في الصيامات كلها.
And if one intends from the night, then retracts his intention before dawn, his retraction is valid in all fasts.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario in voluntary fasts (nawafil) where a person makes an intention at night to fast the next day, but before dawn changes his mind. The ruling is that his retraction is valid and he is no longer obligated to fast. This applies to all types of fasts (voluntary, and according to some, also to obligatory fasts? The text says 'in all fasts' but the context is voluntary as per the earlier discussion. In Hanafi fiqh, for voluntary fasts, the intention can be changed until dawn. For obligatory fasts, the intention must be made before dawn and cannot be changed after, but the text here is from the section on voluntary fasts? The surrounding context mentions 'صيامات كلها' implying all fasts, but the principle is clear: retraction before dawn is effective for voluntary fasts.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujūʿ): retraction or withdrawal of intention
نية (niyyah): intention, a prerequisite for fasting

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must still fast
B) His retraction is valid and he need not fast
C) He must fast but can break later
D) He must make up the fast later

Key Takeaway
Retraction of intention before dawn is valid for all fasts, as intention can be changed before the act begins.

Mere Intention Does Not Invalidate Prayer
Ruling on intending a conflicting act in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that mere intention of a conflicting act does not invalidate prayer and identify the scope of this principle.

Core Idea
Intention alone does not break prayer; action is required.

In Hanafi fiqh, the heart's intention (niyyah) is essential for initiating acts of worship, but once started, a mere change of heart does not nullify the act. This lesson explores a concise rule from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair that clarifies: intending a different prayer while in prayer does not invalidate the original. The key is that intention alone, without corresponding action, is insufficient to break the validity of prayer.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Recall the exact ruling: intending a conflicting act during prayer without action does not invalidate the prayer.
This is a memorization anchor from the text.
2
Identify the key condition
The intention is not accompanied by any physical or verbal action that contradicts the prayer.
Action is the decisive factor.
3
Connect to the broader maxim
This is an application of the principle that acts are judged by intentions, but only when accompanied by action; mere intention after commencement has no effect.
Revisit the qawida 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'.
4
Apply to a similar scenario
If one intends to eat while fasting but does not eat, the fast remains valid.
Parallel ruling from the same principle.
5
Note the mufta bihi
This is the relied-upon position among the Mutakkhirin, as affirmed in Radd al-Muhtar.
Confirm with fatwa hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كذا لونوى فعلا منافياً فی الصلوۃ
And similarly, if one intends an act contrary [to the prayer] while in prayer.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage extends the same principle to prayer: if a person, while performing a particular prayer (e.g., Zuhr), intends a different prayer (e.g., Asr) or any act that contradicts the prayer, his mere intention does not invalidate the original prayer. He must continue the original prayer. This is based on the Hanafi position that intention alone, without corresponding action, does not break the validity of an act of worship.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): intention in the heart

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If during Zuhr prayer you intend to pray Asr instead, but you do not act on it, what happens to your Zuhr prayer?
A) It becomes Asr prayer
B) It becomes invalid
C) It remains valid as Zuhr
D) It becomes a combination prayer

Key Takeaway
Mere intention of a conflicting act, without accompanying action, does not invalidate the prayer.

Five Conditions for Ending Travel by Intention of Residence
Five conditions for a traveler's residence intention to end travel

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions required for a traveler's intention of residence to terminate travel status and apply them to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A traveler becomes a resident only if five conditions are met: cessation of travel, suitable place, unity of place, fifteen days, and independent decision.

A traveler who wishes to end his travel and become a resident must meet specific conditions for his intention (niyyah) to be effective. This lesson presents the five prerequisites laid out in the Hanafi rasm al-mufti tradition, as recorded in Mi'raj al-Diraya. Understanding these conditions is essential for the mufti to correctly determine a person's legal status and prayer obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject
The ruling concerns a traveler (musafir) who intends to cut his travel by forming an intention to reside (iqamah) in a particular place.
2
List the five conditions
From the text, extract the five sharait: cessation of travel, suitability of place, unity of place, duration of fifteen days, and independence of opinion.
3
Explain each condition
Define each condition: cessation means he must actually stop moving; suitability excludes uninhabitable places like sea or island; unity means a single location; duration is a minimum of fifteen days; independence requires that he is not a follower (e.g., wife or servant).
The condition of duration is specific to Hanafi fiqh; other schools may differ.
4
Understand exceptions and implications
Note that if any condition is missing, the intention is invalid and travel continues. The text gives the example of intending residence while still traveling (violation of cessation) or in an unsuitable place.
5
Apply to a contemporary scenario
Given a traveler who stays in a city for 10 days but plans to leave, test each condition to determine if he is now a resident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْنَوَى قَطعَ السَّفْرِ بِالْإِقَامَةِ صَارَ مُقِيمًا، وَبَطَلَ سَفَرُهُ بِخَمْسِ شَرَائِطِ تَرْكَ السَّيْرِ، حَتَّى لَوْ نَوَى الْإِقَامَةَ سَائِرُ الَمْ تَصِحُ، وَصَلَاحِيَةُ الْمَوْضَعِ لِلْإِقَامَةِ فَلَوْ نَوَاهَا فِي بَحْرٍ أَوْ جَزِيْرَةٍ لَمْ تَصِحُ وَإِتِّحَادُ الْمَوْضَعِ وَالْمُدَّةُ وَالْإِسْتِقْلَالُ بِالرَّأَي فَلَا تَصِحُ نِيَّةٌ التَّابِعِ، كَذَا فِي مِعْرَاجِ الدَّرَايَةِ وَإِذَا نَوَى الْمُسَافِرُ الْإِقَامَةَ فِي إِثْنَاءِ صَلَاتِهِ فِي الْوَقْتِ، تَحَوَّلَ فَرْضُهُ إِلَى الأَرْبَعِ سَوَاءٌ نَوَاهَا فِي أَوَّلِهَا، أَوْ فِي أَوْسَطِهَا، أَوْ فِي آخِرِهَا وَسَوَاءٌ كَانَ مُنْفَرِدًا، أَوْ مُقْتَدِيًا، أَوْ مُدْركًا، أَوْ مَسْبُوقًا.
And if one intends to cut travel by intending residence, he becomes a resident, and his travel becomes void with five conditions: cessation of travel, even if he intends residence while traveling it is not valid; suitability of the place for residence, so if he intends it in a sea or island it is not valid; unity of place; duration [of fifteen days]; and independence of opinion, so the intention of a follower is not valid. This is as in Mi'raj al-Diraya. And when a traveler intends residence during his prayer within the time, his obligation changes to four [rak'ahs], whether he intends it at the beginning, middle, or end of the prayer, and whether he is alone, an imam, a follower, or catching up.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on the effect of intending to end travel by forming a residence intention. The conditions are: (1) stopping travel, meaning he must actually cease moving; (2) the chosen location must be suitable for residence, not a sea or uninhabitable island; (3) the place must be a single location; (4) the intended stay must be at least fifteen days; (5) the person must be independent in his decision, not a follower (e.g., a wife or servant). If these conditions are met, the traveler becomes a muqīm (resident) and must perform full prayers. The second part deals with the specific scenario of intending residence while in the middle of a prayer: it transforms his obligation from two rak'ahs to four, regardless of when the intention occurs during the prayer, and regardless of his role in the prayer.

Key Terms
اقامة (iqāmah): intention to reside in a place
شروط (shurūṭ): conditions
استقلال بالرأي (al-istiqlāl bi al-ra'y): independence of opinion, not being a follower

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many conditions are required for a traveler's intention of residence to be valid and terminate his travel status according to the Hanafi school?
A) Three
B) Five
C) Seven
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
A traveler's intention of residence terminates travel only when five conditions are simultaneously met; missing any one preserves the travel status.

Effect of Intending Residence During Prayer
Subsidiary ruling: intending residence during prayer changes obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain what happens when a traveler intends residence during prayer and state the Hanafi ruling on the change of obligation from two to four rak'ahs.

Core Idea
Intending residence during prayer transforms the obligation from two to four rak'ahs, regardless of when the intention occurs or the person's role in the prayer.

A traveler's status can change mid-prayer if he forms a valid intention of residence. This lesson covers the specific rule that when a traveler intends residence while praying within the time, his obligation shifts from two rak'ahs (qasr) to four (tamam). The rule applies uniformly to all participants: imam, follower, alone, or latecomer. Understanding this helps the mufti advise on situations where travel status changes during worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The ruling applies when a traveler (musafir) is engaged in a two-rak'ah prayer (e.g., Zuhr, Asr, Isha) and during the prayer he forms a valid intention to reside (iqamah).
This assumes he is praying within the time of the prayer and his intention meets the five conditions.
2
Note the transformation rule
The obligation (fard) changes from two rak'ahs to four rak'ahs. He must complete four rak'ahs.
3
Identify the timing of intention
The transformation holds whether he intends residence at the beginning, middle, or end of the prayer. This is important for latecomers.
4
Address different roles
The same rule applies whether he is praying alone (munfarid), leading (imam), following (muqtadi), catching up with the imam (mudrik), or joining late (masbuq).
For a masbuq, the intention affects his remaining rak'ahs.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a traveler who intends residence after completing two rak'ahs in a two-rak'ah prayer, he must add two more rak'ahs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْنَوَى قَطعَ السَّفْرِ بِالْإِقَامَةِ صَارَ مُقِيمًا، وَبَطَلَ سَفَرُهُ بِخَمْسِ شَرَائِطِ تَرْكَ السَّيْرِ، حَتَّى لَوْ نَوَى الْإِقَامَةَ سَائِرُ الَمْ تَصِحُ، وَصَلَاحِيَةُ الْمَوْضَعِ لِلْإِقَامَةِ فَلَوْ نَوَاهَا فِي بَحْرٍ أَوْ جَزِيْرَةٍ لَمْ تَصِحُ وَإِتِّحَادُ الْمَوْضَعِ وَالْمُدَّةُ وَالْإِسْتِقْلَالُ بِالرَّأَي فَلَا تَصِحُ نِيَّةٌ التَّابِعِ، كَذَا فِي مِعْرَاجِ الدَّرَايَةِ وَإِذَا نَوَى الْمُسَافِرُ الْإِقَامَةَ فِي إِثْنَاءِ صَلَاتِهِ فِي الْوَقْتِ، تَحَوَّلَ فَرْضُهُ إِلَى الأَرْبَعِ سَوَاءٌ نَوَاهَا فِي أَوَّلِهَا، أَوْ فِي أَوْسَطِهَا، أَوْ فِي آخِرِهَا وَسَوَاءٌ كَانَ مُنْفَرِدًا، أَوْ مُقْتَدِيًا، أَوْ مُدْركًا، أَوْ مَسْبُوقًا.
And if one intends to cut travel by intending residence, he becomes a resident, and his travel becomes void with five conditions: cessation of travel, even if he intends residence while traveling it is not valid; suitability of the place for residence, so if he intends it in a sea or island it is not valid; unity of place; duration [of fifteen days]; and independence of opinion, so the intention of a follower is not valid. This is as in Mi'raj al-Diraya. And when a traveler intends residence during his prayer within the time, his obligation changes to four [rak'ahs], whether he intends it at the beginning, middle, or end of the prayer, and whether he is alone, an imam, a follower, or catching up.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on the effect of intending to end travel by forming a residence intention. The conditions are: (1) stopping travel, meaning he must actually cease moving; (2) the chosen location must be suitable for residence, not a sea or uninhabitable island; (3) the place must be a single location; (4) the intended stay must be at least fifteen days; (5) the person must be independent in his decision, not a follower (e.g., a wife or servant). If these conditions are met, the traveler becomes a muqīm (resident) and must perform full prayers. The second part deals with the specific scenario of intending residence while in the middle of a prayer: it transforms his obligation from two rak'ahs to four, regardless of when the intention occurs during the prayer, and regardless of his role in the prayer.

Key Terms
منفرد (munfarid): one praying alone
مقتد (muqtadi): follower in prayer
مسبوق (masbūq): latecomer who missed part of the prayer
مدرك (mudrik): one who catches the prayer from the beginning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a traveler intends residence during his prayer, when can this intention occur for the obligation to change to four rak'ahs?
A) Only at the beginning of the prayer
B) Only at the middle
C) At any point: beginning, middle, or end
D) Only after the first rak'ah

Key Takeaway
A valid intention of residence during prayer immediately transforms the obligation from two to four rak'ahs, regardless of timing or role.

A Lāḥiq's Intention After Imam Finishes is Ineffective
A rasm al-mufti rule on the lahiq's intention after imam finishes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule regarding a late joiner's intention after the imam finishes and apply it in a fatwa context.

Core Idea
A late joiner's obligation is fixed once the imam finishes; later intention does not change it.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents a rasm al-mufti rule concerning the lahiq (late joiner). The key point is that after the imam completes his prayer, the lahiq's own obligation becomes settled, so any change of intention (e.g., from traveler to resident) after that is ineffective. This is an important principle for muftis when answering questions about travelers praying in congregation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Agent and Timing
The passage specifies the agent: al-lāḥiq (the late joiner), and the timing: after his imam finishes (farāgh imāmihi). This sets the scope of the rule.
2
State the Ruling
The ruling is that the lahiq does not complete his prayer by virtue of a new intention formed after the imam finishes. 'Lā yutimmuhā bi niyyatihā' means his intention is ineffective.
3
Understand the Rationale
The reason (ʿillah) given is 'li istiḥkāmi farḍihi bi farāgh imāmihi' – because his obligation becomes firmly established with the imam's completion. Once established, later intention cannot alter it.
The term 'istiḥkām' implies a binding, fixed status.
4
Confirm the Source
The rule is attested in al-Khulāṣah, a later Hanafi compilation. This establishes its authority within the madhhab's later fatwa literature.
Al-Khulāṣah is among the muʿtabar (reliable) sources for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا الَّلاحِقُ لَا يُتِمُّها بنيتها بعد فراغ إمامه لإسْتحْكامِ فَرْضِهِ بِفَرَاعَ إمَامِهِ، كَذَا فِي الخُلاصَةِ
As for the one who joins late (al-lāḥiq), he does not complete his prayer by virtue of his intention after his imam finishes, because his obligation becomes established with the completion of his imam. Thus in al-Khulāṣah.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage deals with the lahiq, a person who joins the congregation late and misses some rak'ahs. If, during his makeup, he intends residence (iqamah) after his imam has completed the prayer, his intention is ineffective; he must still complete four rak'ahs. The reason is that his obligation (fard) became established as full prayer when his imam finished. This is a technical ruling relevant to travelers praying behind a resident imam or vice versa.

Key Terms
لاحق (lāḥiq): one who joins the prayer late and misses some rak'ahs
فراغ (farāgh): completion, finishing (of the imam's prayer)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because his intention is invalid.
B) Because his obligation is already fixed.
C) Because the imam's prayer is invalid.
D) Because he must follow the imam's intention.

Key Takeaway
A lahiq's obligation is fixed once the imam finishes; later intention does not change it.

Intention Changes Trade Goods to Service Goods
Ruling that intention can change trade goods to service goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that intention alone can convert trade goods into personal service goods, and identify when action is required for the reverse.

Core Idea
Intention can turn trade goods into personal property, but not vice versa without action.

This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents a concise rule on how intention affects the classification of property. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis when determining zakat obligations and legal status of assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage mentions ‘trade goods’ (māl al-tijārah) and ‘service goods’ (māl al-khidmah). Trade goods are held for profit and subject to zakat; service goods are for personal use.
2
Note the one-way effect
If one intends trade goods for personal service, they become service goods by that intention alone. No physical act is required.
3
Contrast the reverse
If one intends personal goods for trade, the text says ‘it would not be effective’ – meaning intention alone is insufficient; a concrete act of offering for sale or entering trade is required.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
Intention (niyyah) has legal weight in property classification to remove a heavier duty (zakat) but not to impose it without external action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ نَوَى بِمَالِ التِّجَارَةِ الْخِدْمَةِ كَانَ لِلْخِدْمَةِ بِالنِّيَّةِ وَلَوْ كَانَ عَلَىٰ عَكْسِهِ لَمْ تُوكَّرْ كَمَا ذَكرَهُ الزَّيْلَعِيُّ
And if one intends with trade-goods [to use them for] service, it becomes for service by intention; and if it were the opposite, it would not be effective, as al-Zaylaʿī mentioned.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This ruling concerns the classification of property. If a person has goods that are considered trade goods (māl al-tijārah) and he intends them for personal service (khidmah), then by that intention they become non-trade property (i.e., for personal use). Conversely, if he intends goods that are for personal use to be for trade, that intention alone is not sufficient to change their status; action (e.g., offering for sale) is required. This illustrates the principle that intention can change the legal classification of property in certain contexts within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مال التجارة (māl al-tijārah): goods kept for trade, subject to zakat upon reaching nisab and passing one year
الخدمة (al-khidmah): service, personal use; property used for personal benefit rather than trade

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone intends trade goods for personal service, what happens?
A) They remain trade goods
B) They become personal property by intention alone
C) They become service goods only after use
D) They become charity

Key Takeaway
Intention alone can change trade goods to personal service goods, but the reverse requires action.

6 min
Effect of Intention on Transgression in Trust
Ruling from al-Fatāwā al-Ẓahīriyyah on intention in transgression of trust

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on transgression in trusts when the trustee intends to repeat the transgression, and apply it in a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
Intention to re-transgress nullifies the removal of transgression — the heart determines liability.

The status of a trustee who misuses property but later stops is not simply based on outward action. The mufti must consider inner intention: if the trustee intends to return to transgression, the original breach of trust persists. This lesson examines a precise ruling from al-Fatāwā al-Ẓahīriyyah and its application in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act under consideration? It is transgression (taʿaddī) on a trust (wadīʿah). The ruling: the transgression does not end if the trustee intends to repeat it after removing it.
The ruling is conditional on intention.
2
Locate the source
Which fatwa collection provides this ruling? It is al-Fatāwā al-Ẓahīriyyah, specifically from the chapter on crimes during iḥrām, though analogized to trusts.
This is a later fatwa source (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
3
Understand the principle
What is the underlying principle? Intention (niyyah) can negate the effect of an outward action. The trustee's intention to return to transgression means the original breach is not truly mended.
This aligns with the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (actions are judged by intentions).
4
Apply to fatwa
How does a mufti use this ruling? When a trustee claims to have ended the transgression, the mufti must inquire about his intention. If the intention to repeat exists, the transgression continues, and the trustee remains liable.
This affects rulings on liability, compensation, and trustworthiness.
5
Distinguish from other cases
How is this ruling different from a case where the trustee repents sincerely? If the intention to repeat is absent, the transgression ends upon removal and sincere repentance.
The presence or absence of intention to return is the decisive factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ أَمَّا نِيَّةُ الْخِيَانَةِ فِي الْوَدِيْعَةِ، فَلَمْ أَرَهَا صريحةً، لكنْ فِي الْفَتَاوى الظَّهِيرِيَّةِ مِنْ جِنايَاتِ الإِحْرَامِ أَنَّ الْمُوْدَعَ إِذَا تَعَلَّى ثُمَّ أَزَالَ التَّعَدّى وَ مِنْ نِيَّتِهِ أَنْ يَعُودَ إِلَيْهِ لَا يَزُولُ التَّعَدّى إنتهى.
And as for the intention of betrayal in a trust (wadīʿah), I have not seen it explicitly [ruled], but in al-Fatāwā al-Ẓahīriyyah, from the chapter on crimes during iḥrām, that if the trustee transgresses, then removes the transgression, but intends to return to it, the transgression does not end. End.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage addresses a nuanced question: if a trustee (mūdaʿ) deliberately uses or misuses the entrusted property (wadīʿah), that is a transgression (taʿaddī). If he later stops the transgression (e.g., returns the property), but internally intends to repeat the transgression in the future, does the original transgression end? According to the Fatāwā Ẓahīriyyah, it does not end because his intention of returning to transgression means he has not truly repented. The ruling is analogized from the chapter on iḥrām violations. This underscores the importance of sincere intention in removing liability.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadīʿah): trust, property left for safekeeping
نية (niyyah): intention
تعدي (taʿaddī): transgression, exceeding limits

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which fatwa collection is cited for the ruling that intention to repeat transgression prevents its removal?
A) al-Hidayah
B) al-Fatāwā al-Ẓahīriyyah
C) al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓāʼir
D) al-Mabsūṭ

Key Takeaway
The removal of transgression in a trust is not complete unless the trustee sincerely repents without intending to repeat.

Effect of Intention on the Latecomer Traveler's Prayer
Explains an exception to the rule of latecomer's intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that once a latecomer's prayer obligation is established (mustaḥkim), subsequent intention to become a resident does not change the mode of completion, and identify the exceptions when it does.

Core Idea
Once the imam finishes, the latecomer's obligation is fixed; later intention cannot alter it.

This lesson examines a specific ruling within the Rasm al-Mufti tradition: when a traveler latecomer (lāḥiq) intends to become a resident after the imam has completed the prayer. The passage clarifies a subtle point: the intention does not affect the obligation because it was already established by the imam's completion. However, there are exceptions when the imam is a resident or the intention occurs before the imam finishes. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that once an act of worship's obligation is determined, subsequent changes in personal status do not retroactively alter it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The latecomer (lāḥiq) is a traveler, the imam is also a traveler. The latecomer intends to become a resident (niyyat iqāmat) after the imam has finished the prayer (said salam).
2
State the ruling
The latecomer does not complete the prayer as a resident (tamam); he completes as a traveler (qasr). His intention does not change the number of rak'ahs.
The ruling is specific to the case where the imam is a traveler and finishes before the latecomer.
3
Analyze the rationale (ta'lil)
The obligation (farḍ) of the latecomer became mustaḥkim (established and firm) at the moment the imam completed the prayer. Subsequent intention cannot affect an already settled obligation.
The concept of istiḥkām is key: once fixed, it does not change.
4
Identify the exceptions
The latecomer will complete as a resident in three cases: (1) when the imam is a resident (muqīm), (2) when the imam is a traveler but the latecomer intends residence before the imam finishes, and (3) [a third case not detailed here but derived from fuller texts].
These exceptions show that timing and imam's status are critical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح : مذکورہ مسئلہ میں لاحق کی ایک صورت کا بیان ہے جو پہلے مسئلہ سے مختلف ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مقتدی لائق مسافر ہو اور امام بھی مسافر ہو اور امام کے فارغ ہونے کے بعد وہ مسافر لاحق مقتدی اقامت کی نیت کرلے تو اقامت کی نیت کرنے کی وجہ سے وہ لاحق مسافر جو مقتدی ہے اتمام نہیں کرے گا کیونکہ امام کے فارغ ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا فرض مستحکم ہو گیا۔
-
ولم یک لاحقاً و تحته قال الشامى اما اللاحق اذا ادرک اولی الصلوة و الامام مسافر الى قوله فلا يتغير في حق الامام (شامی زکریا ج ۲، ص: ۶۰۵) البتہ لاحق تین صورتوں میں اقامت کی نیت کی وجہ سے اتمام کرے گا (۲/۱ ۱ ) جب کہ امام مقیم ہو ( ۳) امام مسافر ہو اور امام کے فراغت سے پہلے لاحق مسافر اقامت کی نیت کر لے۔
Explanation: The aforementioned issue describes one case of the latecomer that differs from the previous issue, and it is that if the follower is a traveler and the imam is also a traveler, and after the imam finishes, that traveling latecomer follower intends to become a resident, then due to the intention of residence, that latecomer traveler who is the follower will not complete (the prayer as a resident) because his obligation became established due to the imam's completion. - And it is not [applicable] as a latecomer, and under it al-Shami said: As for the latecomer who catches the beginning of the prayer while the imam is a traveler, up to his saying: so it does not change concerning the imam (Shami, Zakariyya, vol. 2, p. 605). However, the latecomer will complete (as a resident) due to the intention of residence in three cases: (1) when the imam is a resident, (2) when the imam is a traveler and the latecomer traveler intends residence before the imam finishes.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Rasm al-Mufti genre, explaining an exception to the general rule of how a latecomer (lahaq) in prayer is affected by intention. The base rule: if a traveler follower missed a rak'ah and becomes a latecomer, his prayer is based on the imam's status. Here, the author clarifies that if the imam finishes first, the latecomer's obligation is already established; thus, a subsequent intention to become resident does not change his duty to complete as a traveler. The three exceptions quoted from al-Shami show when the intention to reside does affect the latecomer's completion: when the imam is a resident, or when the latecomer intends residence before the imam finishes. The passage emphasizes the principle that once an obligation is settled, later intentions do not alter its nature.

Key Terms
لاحق (lāḥiq): A follower who joins the prayer after the first rak'ah and missed one or more rak'ahs; he follows the imam's ruling initially but becomes independent after the imam's salam.
اقامت کی نیت (iqāmat kī niyyat): Intention to become a resident (muqīm), i.e., to stay at a place for at least 15 days, which changes the traveler's rulings to those of a resident.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the discussed ruling, when does the intention to become resident affect the latecomer traveler's prayer completion?
A) When the intention is made after the imam finishes
B) When the imam is a resident
C) When the intention is made silently
D) When the latecomer is also a resident

Key Takeaway
The latecomer traveler's obligation becomes fixed upon the imam's completion; later intention to reside does not change the mode of completion.

The Rule on Particularizing a General Oath by Intention
Fatwa rule on specification of general oaths by intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi fatwa rule on particularizing a general oath by distinguishing between diyanatan (religious) and qada'an (judicial) acceptance, and identify the exception for those under duress.

Core Idea
A general oath can be specified by intention only religiously, not in court—except under oppression.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair presents a critical rasm al-mufti rule: when someone makes a general oath (e.g., 'every woman I marry is divorced') and later claims he intended a specific subset, how does the law treat that intention? The Hanafi school makes a key distinction between religious validity (diyanatan) and judicial enforceability (qada'an). This lesson unpacks that rule, the dispute with al-Khassaf, and the practical fatwa approach when a mufti encounters such a case, especially under duress.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the ruling of the Apparent Madhhab (Zahir al-Riwayah): a general oath can be specified by intention only religiously (diyanatan), not in court (qada'an).
2
Recognize the opposing view
Al-Khassaf held that such specification is also accepted judicially. Note his reasoning: oaths are based on intentions.
3
Understand the diyanatan/qada'an distinction
Diyanatan means valid before Allah—the person may act accordingly. Qada'an means a judge accepts it. The distinction prevents fraud while honoring true intention religiously.
4
Identify the exceptional concession
For someone forced by oppressors to swear, al-Khassaf's view can be followed as a relief. This is an exception to the general fatwa.
5
Apply the rule to a new case
Given a scenario of a general oath and a later claimed specification, determine the outcome diyanatan vs. qada'an and whether the duress exception applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَصِحْ فِي ظَاهِرِ الْمَذْهَبِ خِلَافاً لِلْخَصَّافِ وَ كَذَا مَنْ غَصَبَ دَرَاهِمَ إِنْسَانِ فَلَمَّا خَلْفَهُ الْخَصْمُ عَامَّا نَوَى خَاصًا وَ مَا قَالَهُ الْخَصَّافُ مَخْلَصٌ لِمَنْ حَلَّفَهُ ظَالِمٌ وَ وَالْفَتْوَىٰ عَلَىٰ ظَاهِرِ الْمَذْهَبِ فَمَنْ وَقَعَ فِي يَدِ الظُّلَمَةِ وَ ُأخِذَ بِقَوْلِ الْخَصَّافِ فَلَا بَأْسَ بِهِ كَذَا فِي الْوَالْوَ الْحِيَّة . وَ الْحَاصِلُ ان نِيَّةَ تَخْصِيصِ الْعَامِّ تَصِحُّ فِي ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَةِ دِيَانَةً فَقَطْ وَ عِنْدَ الْخَصَّافِ تَصِحُّ قَضَاءً أَيْضاً وَ هٰذَا إِذَا كَانَ الْعَامُّ مَذْكُوراً. كُلُّ امْرَأَةٍ أَتَزَوَّجُهَا: فَلَوْ قَالَ كُلُّ امْرَأَةٍ أَتَزَوَّجُهَا فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ ثُمَّ قَالَ نَوَيْتُ مِنْ بَلَدِ كَذَا لَا يُصَدَّقُ قَضَاءً إِلَى قَوْلِهِ خِلَافاً لِلْخَصَّافِ.
It is valid in the apparent madhhab, contrary to al-Khassaf. Likewise, one who usurped someone's dirhams and then when the opponent made him swear generally, he intended specifically. And what al-Khassaf said is a relief for one whom an oppressor makes swear. And the fatwa is on the apparent madhhab. So whoever falls into the hands of oppressors and takes al-Khassaf's saying, there is no harm. Thus in [book reference]. And the summary: The intention to particularize the general is valid in Zahir al-Riwayah only religiously (diyanatan), but according to al-Khassaf it is also valid judicially (qada'an). This is when the general term is mentioned. 'Every woman I marry:' So if one says 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' then says 'I intended from such a city,' he is not believed judicially up to his saying, contrary to al-Khassaf.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage establishes a rasm rule for muftis: when someone makes a general oath (e.g., 'every woman I marry is divorced') and later claims he intended a specific subset (e.g., women of a certain city), the Hanafi madhhab (Zahir al-Riwayah) accepts this intention only on the religious level (diyanatan), meaning it is valid before Allah and the person may act accordingly, but not in a court of law (qada'an). The ruling is based on the principle that the apparent general meaning takes precedence in judicial matters. However, Imam al-Khassaf (a later Hanafi scholar) held that such intention is also accepted judicially if the general term was explicitly mentioned. The same applies to a case where someone usurped dirhams and then, when forced to swear generally by an oppressor, intended a specific amount. The fatwa for the general public is on Zahir al-Riwayah, but if someone is under duress from oppressors, they may follow al-Khassaf's view as a concession. The example of marriage illustrates that if someone says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and later specifies a city, a judge will not accept that specification (qada'an), but the person can still marry women from that city religiously (diyanatan). Al-Khassaf would accept it in court as well, resolving the difficulty.

Key Terms
ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَة (Zahir al-Riwayah): The established transmitted rulings from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
دِيَانَةً (diyanatan): Religiously, i.e., valid before Allah but not enforced by a judge.
قَضَاءً (qada'an): Judicially, i.e., enforceable by a court of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Zahir al-Riwayah, if a person swears 'every woman I marry is divorced' and later claims he intended only women of a specific city, how is his intention treated?
A) It is accepted judicially (qada'an) and religiously (diyanatan).
B) It is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not judicially (qada'an).
C) It is accepted neither religiously nor judicially.
D) It is accepted judicially but not religiously.

Key Takeaway
The intention to particularize a general oath is valid religiously but not in court, with a concession for those under duress to follow al-Khassaf's view.

Diyanatan Acceptance of Intention in Oaths of Manumission
Explains when intention to specify slaves is accepted diyanatan and when it is not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on diyanatan acceptance of intention in oaths of manumission, distinguishing between plausible and arbitrary specifications.

Core Idea
In oaths of manumission, only intentions supported by custom are accepted even religiously; arbitrary distinctions are rejected.

When a person makes a sweeping oath about all his slaves, can his later intention narrow it? The Hanafi school accepts such specification religiously (diyanatan) only if it aligns with natural custom, like gender. Skin color distinctions are not accepted even before Allah. This lesson examines the rule and its rationale, which is essential for muftis issuing fatwas in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and the claim
Recognize the scenario: a person says 'every slave I own is free' and later claims he intended only a subset (e.g., males, blacks).
The oath is general (amm), and the intention is an attempt to specify (takhsis).
2
Apply the rule of diyanatan acceptance
Determine whether the intention is accepted religiously (duyyina) or rejected. The text distinguishes two outcomes: gender specification is accepted; skin color specification is not.
Diyanatan means the person is believed in his claim before Allah, but not enforced in court.
3
Analyze the rationale based on custom
Gender is a natural category commonly associated with the term 'slave', so the intention is plausible. Skin color is arbitrary and not customarily linked, so the intention is rejected.
The rule is built on the maxim 'al-‘urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative).
4
Understand the limitation on generalizing a specific term
The author notes that the opposite operation—generalizing a specific term by intention (ta'mim al-khass)—is not accepted and has been unseen by him. This would add to the wording without basis.
This contrasts with takhsis (specifying a general term) which can be accepted if plausible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ كُلَّ مَمْلُوْكٍ أَمْلِكُهُ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ وَقَالَ عَنَيْتُ بِهِ الرِّجَالُ دُونَ النِّسَاءِ دُيِّنَ بِخِلَافِ مَا لَوْ قَالَ نَوَيْتُ السُّوْدَ دُونَ الْبَيْضِ أوْ بِالْعَكْسِ لَمْ يُصَدَّقْ دِيَانَةً أَيْضًا كَقَوْلِهِ نَوَيْتُ النِّسَاءَ دُونَ الرِّجَالِ وَ الْفَرْقُ بَيَّنَّاهُ فِي الشَّرْحِ مِنْ بَابِ الْيَمِيْنِ بِالطَّلَاقِ وَالْعَتَاقِ وَ أَمَّا تَعْمِيمُ الْخَاصِّ بِالنِّيَّةِ فَلَمْ أَرَهُ الْآنَ.
And if he said 'every slave I own is free' and said 'I meant by it the males, not the females,' he is believed (diyanatan). Contrary to if he said 'I intended the black without the white' or vice versa, he is not believed diyanatan either, like his saying 'I intended the females without the males.' And we explained the difference in the commentary in the chapter of oath by divorce and manumission. As for generalizing the specific by intention, I have not seen it now.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced rule for muftis regarding oaths of manumission. If someone swears 'every slave I own is free' and later claims he intended only male slaves, his claim is accepted religiously (diyanatan) but not in court (qada'an) — this is based on the prior lesson. However, the key contribution here is the distinction: if the person claims he intended only black slaves as opposed to white, or a similarly arbitrary category, then even diyanatan his claim is rejected. The reason is that using 'slave' generally includes both genders, and specifying 'males' is a plausible restriction since gender is a natural and common distinction. But skin color is not inherently connected to the word 'slave' — such a specification would be arbitrary and not supported by custom ('urf). The same applies if he intended females over males. The author notes that the full difference has been explained in the commentary on the chapter of oaths of divorce and manumission. Finally, the author remarks that the opposite operation — generalizing a specific term by intention (e.g., saying 'my slave is free' and intending all slaves) — has not been encountered in the sources. That would amount to adding to the wording, which is not allowed by custom, so it would not be accepted even diyanatan, as the author implies.

Key Terms
دُيِّنَ (duyyina): He is believed religiously (diyanatan). That is, his claim is accepted before Allah and he may act accordingly, but not enforced in court.
تَعْمِيمُ الْخَاصِّ بِالنِّيَّةِ (ta'mim al-khass bi al-niyyah): The attempt to extend a specific term (e.g., 'my slave') to include others (e.g., all slaves) through mental intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted judicially and religiously.
B) It is accepted religiously but not judicially.
C) It is not accepted even religiously.
D) It is not accepted at all, neither religiously nor judicially.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi iftaa, an intention to specify an oath's scope is accepted religiously only if it aligns with custom; arbitrary distinctions are rejected even before Allah.

Specifying General Oath Words by Intention: The Role of Custom and Diyaanatan Acceptance
Ruling on intention to specify general oath words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a person's intention to specify a general oath word is accepted diyaanatan and apply the principle that ta'mim al-kaas is not valid.

Core Idea
In oaths, intention can specify a general word only when it aligns with a recognized subcategory; arbitrary distinctions are rejected even diyaanatan.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym and Imam Shami clarifies a nuanced boundary in Hanafi fatwa practice: when does a swearer's internal intention override the apparent meaning of his words? By examining cases of slave emancipation oaths, we learn the critical role of custom ('urf) in determining the validity of an intention. This lesson will equip you to distinguish between acceptable and unacceptable specifications in oaths, a skill essential for issuing fatwa in marital and financial disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of specification
The passage gives examples: specifying men vs women (accepted diyaanatan) and black vs white (not accepted). Note the difference: the first is a recognized subcategory, the second is arbitrary.
2
Distinguish diyaanatan from qada'an
Diyaanatan means the person is believed in religious matters (before Allah) but not legally enforced. Qada'an is court enforcement based on apparent evidence.
This distinction is fundamental in Hanafi fiqh, allowing for inner truth without disrupting legal certainty.
3
Apply the custom ('urf) test
The acceptance of intention hinges on whether the specified subcategory is recognized in the word's usage. Men and women are distinct; black and white are arbitrary and not rooted in the word's meaning.
Custom must be considered when evaluating plausibility.
4
Recognize the prohibition of ta'mim al-kaas
The passage states that generalizing a specific word by intention (making a word that is specific become general) is not found in sources and is invalid. Imam Shami explains it is an addition to the wording, which even custom does not allow, so intention cannot justify it.
This is the opposite operation: trying to expand meaning rather than restrict.
5
Trace the authority level
Ibn Nujaym reports he has not seen any ruling on ta'mim al-kaas. Imam Shami then derives the rule through reasoning (addition invalid by custom, even more so by intention). This is fatwa-level reasoning from mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ قَالَ كُلَّ مَمْلُوْفٍ أَمْلِكُهُ فَهُوَ حُرِّ وَقَالَ عَنَيْتُ بِهِ الرِّجَالُ دُونَ النِّسَاءِ دُيِّنَ بِخِلَافِ مَالَوْ قَالَ نَوَيْتُ السُّوْدَ دُونَ الْبَيْضِ أوْ بِالْعَكْسِ لَمْ يُصَدَّقَ دِيَانَةً أَيْضًا كَقَوْلِهِ نَوَيْتُ النِّسَاءَ دُونَ الرِّجَالِ وَ الْفَرْقُ بَيَّنَّاهُ فِي الشَّرْحِ مِنْ بَابِ الْيَمِيْنِ بِالطَّلَاقِ وَالْعَتَاقِ وَ أَمَّا تَعْمِيمُ الْخَاصٌ بِالنِّيَّةِ فَلَمْ أَرَهُ الْآنَ.

اور اگر کہا کہ ہر وہ غلام جو میری ملکیت میں ہے وہ آزاد ہے اور کہا کہ میری مراد مردوں سے تھی نہ کہ عورتوں سے تو دیانت اس کی تصدیق کی جائے گی بر خلاف اس کے کہ وہ کہے کہ میں نے کالوں کی نیت کی تھی نہ کہ گوروں کی یا اس کا برعکس تو دیا یہ بھی اس کی تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی جیسے وہ یوں کہے کہ میری نیت عورتوں کی تھی نہ کہ مردوں کی اور فرق ہم نے کنز کی شرح باب اسیمین بالطلاق والعتاق میں بیان کر دیا اور رہی خاص کو عام کرنے کی نیت تواسے میں نے اب تک نہیں دیکھا ہے۔ تشریح : کسی نے کل مملوک لی حر کہا تو اس کے تمام غلام اور باندیاں آزاد ہو جائیں گے اب اگر وہ یہ کہے که میری صرف مرد غلاموں کی نیت تھی تو دیانہ اس کی بات کا اعتبار ہوگا قضاء اس کی باتوں کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا۔ کل مملوک لی حر عتق عبيده القن و امهات اولاده و مکاتبه (الحرج ۴، ص: ۳۴۵) خاص الفاظ کونیت کے ذریعہ عام کرنے کا حکم :
خاص الفاظ کو نیت کے ذریعہ عام کر سکتے ہیں یا نہیں ؟ تو اس سلسلہ میں علامہ ابن نجیم المصری فرماتے ہیں کہ میں نے اس کا کہیں حکم نہیں دیکھا علامہ شامی فرماتے ہیں کہ عام کرنے سے مراد لفظ پر زیادتی ہے اور جب عرف کی وجہ سے لفظ پر زیادتی درست نہیں ہے تو نیت سے بدرجہ اولی تقسیم نہ ہوگی کیونکہ عرف نیست کے برخلاف ظاہر ہے۔ قلت والظاهر ان تعميمه من الزيادة على اللفظ و إذا لم تصح الزيادة عليه بالعرف فلا تصح بالنية الأولى لأن العرف ظاهر بخلاف النية. (شامی ج ۵ ص: ۵۸۵)
And if he said: 'Every slave I possess is free', and he said: 'I meant by it the men, not the women', he is believed diyaanatan, unlike if he said: 'I intended the black ones, not the white ones', or the opposite, he is not believed even diyaanatan, just as his saying: 'I intended the women, not the men'. And the difference we have explained in the commentary in the chapter of oath by divorce and emancipation. As for generalizing a specific [word] by intention, I have not seen it now. Translation: And if he said: 'Every slave I own is free' and said: 'My intention was men, not women', then his statement is accepted diyaanatan. Contrary to if he says: 'I intended blacks, not whites' or vice versa, then his statement is not accepted even diyaanatan, like if he says: 'My intention was women, not men'. And the difference we have explained in the commentary of Kanz in the chapter of oath by divorce and emancipation. As for the intention to generalize a specific [word], I have not seen it yet. Commentary: If someone says 'kullu mamlookin liya hurr', all his male and female slaves become free. Now if he says: 'My intention was only male slaves', then diyaanatan his word is considered, but qada'an his word is not considered. 'Kullu mamlookin liya hurr' includes his own slaves, ummahat awlad, and mukatab. (al-Harj 4, p.345) Ruling on generalizing specific words through intention: Can specific words be generalized through intention? Allamah Ibn Nujaym al-Misri says: I have not seen its ruling anywhere. Allamah Shami says: To generalize means to add to the word. And when addition to the word is not valid due to custom, then through intention it is all the more invalid, because custom is apparent in contrast to intention. I say: And the apparent is that its generalization is an addition to the word. And if addition to the word is not valid by custom, then it is not valid by intention a fortiori, because custom is apparent unlike intention. (Shami vol.5 p.585)
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the acceptance of intention in oaths of emancipation. It distinguishes between cases where the swearer's specification aligns with a recognized subcategory (men vs. women) versus arbitrary distinctions (black vs. white). In the first case, the intention is accepted diyaanatan (religiously) but not qada'an (in court); in the second, it is not accepted even diyaanatan. The passage also addresses the reverse issue: cannot use intention to generalize a specific word (e.g., making 'slave' include both genders when the word is general). Imam Shami clarifies that this would be adding to the wording, which is invalid even by intention if custom does not support it.

Key Terms
خاص (khaas): A specific word with a limited meaning, as opposed to general ('amm).
عام (amm): A general word that covers all its members.
تعميم الخاص (ta'mim al-kaas): Generalizing a specific word by intention, i.e., expanding its meaning beyond its apparent scope. The text states this is not valid and not seen.
ديانة (diyaanatan): In religious matters; the person's claim is believed before Allah but not enforced in court.
قضاء (qada'an): In court; legal enforcement based on apparent evidence and testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why in the case of 'kullu mamlookin liya hurr' is the person believed diyaanatan when he says he meant only male slaves, but not if he says he meant only black slaves?

B) The word 'mamlook' includes both men and women by default, but black and white are not distinguished in the word.

D) The court accepts intentions only when the swearer is oppressed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, intention to specify an oath word is accepted diyaanatan only when the specification is plausible and consistent with custom; otherwise it is rejected, and generalizing a specific word by intention is invalid altogether.
